A LARGE BONUS.
The Company has recently forwarded to the holder of policy No. 278,127 a cheque for
£1,099 13s. 6d. in payment of the cash va ue of the Bonus for 1891, the
Policy being for 10,000, and issued in 1886, on the five year distribution plan. This
return is equal to an annual cash bonus of £2 4s. for each £lOO insured. Many of
these i o icies are reaching the bonus period with results very gratifying to the Insured.
The total payments to Policy-holders to December, 1890, amounted to £63,469,822,
of which upwards of £17,400,000 were bonus payments—more than twice the amount of
Bonuses paid by any other Company.

SYDNEY.
Tlxe Influenza Fiend.!
For such must be considered the'living organism which by its rapid propagation has spread disease and death
throughout the limits of the civiliaed world. The scientists of the period have attributed the prevalence and
transmission of this influenza epidemic to the action of quickly propagating germs, and although keen-eyed and
learned men in England, France and Germany have been searching for this microscopic mischief with unstinting
labour, they have not yet succeeded in isolating the individual germ and learning his habits, his food and his
poison. In the meantime, with no uncertain note, leading physicians in London have recognised in the Austra-
lian Oil of Eucalyptus a germicide that has been pronounced both a preventive of and a cure for Influenza.
In the Lancet of 18th January, 1890, Dr. W. Stephenson Richmond, of Kensington, London, writes; —
“As to the treatment, preventive and curative, I believe enough has not been said about the former.
Believing influenza to be due to a germ, and generally preceded by catarrh, I assume preventive medicine should
aim at destruction of the germ and relief of the congestion. I believe for the former there is nothing to surpass
eucalyptus oil during the present epidemic. I believe it is absolutely preventive, and my experience and observa-
tion of it are not confined to the present epidemic. Eucalyptus destroys the germs, and added to ammonia, which
stimulates the local circulation, forms the.best inhalent for the prevention of the disease X yet know of. I think
it vastly superior to menthol.”—(Signed), W. Stephenson Richmond, M.R.C.S.
In February, 1890, Dr. Brendon Curgenven writes in the Lancet.
To the Editor of the Lancet : “I can fully endorse the experience of Mr. Stephenson Richmond in the use of
Eucalyptus as a preventive of influenza, at recommended by him in the Lancet of 18th January. I have from the
beginning of the epidemic used Eucalyptus disinfectant spray on the handkerchief as a perfume by day, and
sprinkled on the bed pillow for inhalation at night, and have found it most effectual in preventing influenza. In
the cases of old people and invalids, I have directed the spray to be used in their rooms at intervals in the day
and sprinkled on their pillows at night, and I am convinced I have saved many from the disease, when their
servants, who have not taken these precautions, have had it. I have not had the disease myself, although I have
attended upwards of 200 cases. I have experienced the effects of a large dose of the poison several times after
visiting houses where there were many cases. I felt considerable nausea, like that of sea-sickness, and such dizzi-
ness that I could scarcely walk straight, together with great languor. All these symptoms passed away on in-
haling the Eucalyptus with strong inspirations until its warmth could be felt in the chest. It will as effectually
destroy the poisons of other infectious diseases and fevers, and I hope to bring the subject of the use of eucalyptus
in infectious diseases before the Epidemiological Society on 12th March, when I shall prove its powerful effect on
the scarlet fever poison.”—l am, sir, yours faithfully, (Signed), J. Brendon Curoenven, Bayswater, Ist February
1890. *
Bosisto’s “ Parrot ” Brand is both curative and preventive. Its antiseptic principle immedi-
ately destroys all poisonous germs.
Mr. Joseph Bosisto was the first to distil and introduce eucalyptus oil into Europe, and his oil may be known
by the trade-mark, the “ Parrot ” brand. He first exhibited his eucalyptus oil at the Paris Exhibition of 1855
then again in the London Exhibition of 1861, receiving the highest houours for his exhibits. In 1886, the Society
of Arts, Manufactures, and Commerce of London (incorporated by Royal Charter in 18X7, and established in 1754)
conferred on Mr. Bosisto the silver medal of the society in recognition of his services to medicine and the arts in
introducing the eucalyptus of the Auslralian flora into the commerce of Europe.
From the earliest years of its introduction into Europe Bosisto’s oil of eucalyptus has filled the European
demand, and will be found in the dispensaries of all the hospitals, and in the laboratories of all the leading
chemists of Europe.
Bosisto’s “ PARROT ” brand Eucalyptus Oil has as trade mark a parrot on the top of the wrapper. See you
get this from your chemist, and so avoid imitations.

adjoining, and its level land proves it to be eminently
adapted for residential purposes. Nature seems to have
lavished all her varied charms on BUCKINGHAM PARK,
which is, without exception, the CREAM or PICKED
SPOT on the BLUE MOUNTAINS.
BUCKINGHAM PARK ESTATE
Is beautifully surveyed. The Allotments are unusually
large— from 100 up to 180 feet frontages, with long depths
—thus enabling buyers to have Garden, Tennis Lawns,
etc., if they so desire. In fact, it may be truly said, there
is no finer Residential Land on the Mountains.

combined with unswerving faithfulness to his

over FORTY YEARS, attained a well-deserved
pre-eminence in their treatment. His practice
embraces diseases which probably cause more un-
happiness than any other class of troubles that
afflict mankind.
YOUNG MEN
who suffer from the results of early follies, trans-
gressions or excesses of more nature age, bladder
difficulties, a whitish or dark ropy sediment in

smarting sensation, Headaches, Drowsiness during

in the back. Liver or kidney complaints, dreams,

weak stomach, yellow or blood-shot eyes, impure
blood, hasty, uncontrollable temper, hacking

SATURDAY, DECEMBER 5, 1891.
A Freetrade Tariff.
During the year that is xxow approaching its
close, business has been prosperous at the
New South Wales Customs House, and the
takings under the alleged Fx-eetrade tariff
have been xnxusually good. It is somewhat
of an anomaly that there should have beexx
any such takings at all, for if Freetrade
xxxeaxxs anything—which we doubt—thexx
there should be no tariff whatsoever-, and no
Customs House, axxd no revenue from duties
of any kind, but that is a mere detail. It is
also somewhat of an anomaly that the
alleged Freetx-ade press should have re-
joiced over the amount extracted from the
consumer, by this same iniquitous tariff,
seeing that it is against its prixxciple—
if it has any principles that there
should be a tariff. But while these jour-
nals pointed to the £2,000,000 per annum
wrested from the Victorian people under
Px-otection as a black and sixxful circum-
stance, they cheered wildly over the
£2,000,000 wrested from the people of New
South Wales under Fx-eetrade, and poixxted
to it as a great and solid evidence of pros-
perity. And while they have never wearied
of complaining that the £2,000,000 levied in
Victoria is an oppressive addition to the
poor man’s burdens, they never once men-
ioned that the same amount levied in New
South Wales is an addition to anybody’s
burdens ; also, when the amount re-
ceived in Customs’ duties in Melbourne
showed signs of increasing they said
that a further outrage was being heaped
on a down - trodden people, and when
the Customs duties of N.S.W. increased in
like manner they stated that the great cause
of liberty was looking up. This strangely
anomalous proceeding is still going on, and
the papers which supported Pakkes because
he levied about £2,000,000 per annum and
called it Freetrade, now curse Dibbs with a
dreadful curse because he proposes to charge
just about the same amount and call it Pro-
tection. Also they keep on demonstrating
with a weary iteration that the money Dibbs
raises will come out of the pockets of the
people, and never once remark that the
same amount, when Parkes raised it, came
out of exactly the same pockets.
# # *
In fact, when they turn on their tears and
lamentations over the fact that the province
is going to have a tariff, the Daily Tele-
graph and the Herald cease to rejoice over
the takings of the New South Wales Customs
department, and invariably forget that
New South Wales has got any tariff’ at all.
It always had one, none the less. It is
called a “revenue tariff,” and is understood
to bo levied only as a matter of necessity,
but the one point which affects the people is
that it is levied. Whatever it is called they
have to pay it, and whether it is described as
a revenue tariff or a dado or a megatherium
the result is the same. The difference
between the Freetrader and the Pro-
tectionist is simply that the former says
there should not be a tariff, and levies one ;
while the latter says that there should be a
tariff, and levies one. Also, the Protec-
tionist levies his tariff on goods that can be
made in the country, and thereby hopes to
raise money and foster local interests at the
same time ; while the Freetrader piles his
exactions upon goods that cannot be made in
the country, and thereby only enhances
prices without fostering any industry at all.
If the Protectionist is deceived in his views,
and his system does not assist industry in
any way, his scheme then leads to exactly
the same results as that of his opponent : it
produces revenue to an equal extent, and
leaves things as they were. The distinction
between the two policies, as exemplified in
New South Wales and the adjacent province,
is that, while taxing the people to a similar
amount, Protection tries to render some
benefit in return, and alleged Freetrade
does not. If Premier Dibbs adopts the 25
per cent, tariff now in fo;ce in Yictoi’ia, the
cornstalk race will keep on paying the
same old oppressive sum per annum which
it pays at present ; therefore, it will lose
nothing, and even if it gains nothing it will
be no worse off. The awful rage of the
bigoted Foreign-trade party is roused merely
because some two-thirds of the nation
desires, in its financial extremity, to try a
remedy which will cost absolutely naught,
and which may do a vast amount of good,
and therefore it calls on the other third to
rise as one man and die lighting for the old
tariff incubus that avowedly does no good
to anybody, and was never intended to.
# # #
Through much hard Foreign-trade lying a
large section of the people of New South
Wales has been taught to regard Protection
merely as a system which puts on duties,
and the fact that it is quite as much a system
which takes duties off is altogether kept out
of sight. In like manner Freetrade, as
px-eached and practised at Sydney, is vaunted
as a great device which takes off duties, and
the circumstance that it also piles them oxx
with a lavish hand is never mentioned. The
duty of 3d. per lb. levied in New South
Wales oxx unmanufactured coffee and cocoa
is strictly a Freetrade duty, and under a
scientific system of Protection it would be
abolished on the ground that it merely
enhanced the price of one of the xiecessaries
of life, without encouraging any possible
industry in return. The Fx-cotrade systexxx,
meanwhile, cares nothing at all as to whether
the prices of the necessaries of life ax’o
enhanced or not —in fact, its chief business
is to enhance pi-ices. The duty upoxx tea
comes into the same categoxy ; it does xxo
good and merely increases tlie cost to the
consumer. The tax of £3 per ton levied
upon rice is a gigantic addition to the price
of a valuable article of food, and as thex-e is
no reasonable px-ospect of growing rice in
New South Wales, this item of the great
Fx-eetrade tariff is also passed oxx to the
retail buyer as an absolutely unprofitable
exaction. Sago is taxed to the extent of
xxearly £lO per ton ; so are tapioca and semo-
and there is a ponderous duty oxx
sugar-all of which, under a system of pure
axxd unadulterated Protection, would be
admitted free 6n the gx-ound that the duties
xxow levied, upoxx them can assist no local
industry whatever, and are oxxly a bald and
naked tax upon the workman’s wages. The
same rules apply to the tax of 3s. 4d. per
cwt. on treacle and molasses—of fid. per
gallon on naphtha and kex-osene - and of ss.
per cwt. on glucose, all of which raise prices
without furnishing cmployxxieut to a single
human being save the Customs-officer. The
chai’ge that is heaved with such wearisome
moxxotony at Protection is that it is simply a
scheme whereby a certaiix percentage is
charged to the consumer, but if the duties
levied in the name of Fx-eetx-ade are not
charged to the coxxsumer, then to whom, in
all the wide world, are they chax-ged ? The
same principle applies to theexcise of 14s. per
gallon upon spirits, of threepence per gallon
on beer, and of Is. 3d. and 2s. (id per lb.,
respectively, upon tobacco and cigars, all of
New South Wales manufacture. They are
paid, and the buyer is beyond a doubt the
only person who can pay them, and there-
fore the curse that is levelled against Protec-
tion fits Freetrade just as well. It is a
ready-made or slop curse, and will fit almost
anything.
*- * #
Apart from these, however, which are
more distinctly Freetrade imposts, having
no effect whatever save to take money out
of the pockets of the poorer classes. New
South Wales is well off for miscellaneous
duties. There is a heavy tax on iron,
which, in Victoria, is admitted free. There
are taxes on beer, ale, porter, cider, bacon,
hams, biscuits, butter, candles, cement,
cheese, chicory, corn-flour, maizena, wire,
confectionery, essences, fish, fruits, jellies,
jams, condensed milk, opium, paints, varnish,
powder and shot, galvanised iron, dynamite,
litho fracteur, salt, sarsaparilla, spirits of all
descriptions, timber, sashes, doors, wines,
and other articles. Many of these duties
would naturally lead to the establishment of
new industries, but wherever it seemed likely
that any particular impost would indirectly
support some industry and furnish employ-
ment to the local population, then some
other impost was carefully put on to crush it
out. The duty on imported sweets might
have heljied along the local confectioners,
and supplied work and wages for many
individuals who wanted both very badly ; so
a duty was placed on sugar and essences—
the necessary raw material of the trade—to
extinguish them, and therefore sugar and
confectionery are both materially iucreased
in price without any good results ensuing.
There was danger, at one time, that the
manufactiu’e of machinery and iron-work
might start in the province, therefore taxes
were piled up on paints, varnish, oils, and
other sundries employed in the business to
suppress this nefarious tendency to advance-
ment, and ready-made machinery was ad-
mitted free. This was Protection in its
baldest form—Protection, that is, to the
European cheap artisan, and against the
Australian. A heavy duty was placed
on foreign spirits, and, lest the distil-
ling business might get a footing, an
equally ponderous impost was placed on local
spirits. The charge upon doors, window-
sashes, and shutters looked as if it might
boom the woodware factories of New South
Wales, consequently timber was taxed to
keep'the business in a proper state of sub-
jection. Everywhere the rule of the
Foreign-traders was, when they saw a new
industry, to hit it, and this rule has been
adhered to so religiously, and the beauties of
it have been expounded so long and so per-
sistently by the Daily Telegraph, and the
Herald, and the Echo, and the Evening
News, that the ancient superstition still has
some few adherents in New South Wales,
and though dead it persists in festering
above-ground, and obstinately refuses to be
buried.
# # #
Another peculiar feature of the New South
Wales “Freetrade tariff” is that it is in the
strictest sense of the term a charge upon the
poor. There is no impost on watches,
diamond rings, brooches, gold bangles,
pianos, organs, carpets, carriages, furniture
—it is only the expensive variety of furni-
ture that is imported, and imported furniture
is the luxury of the rich ; the poor man’s
deal table is heavily taxed under the heading
of “dressed timber”—optical instruments,
marble, pictures and paintings, silver plate,
or any other single article that is the exclu-
sive luxury of the capitalist. This intense
anxiety on the part of the wealthy Foreign-
trade law-makers to prevent any tax being
levied which might-, by any chance, increase
the price of their particular indulgences, and
their fixed resolution to shunt all possible
charges on to the poor, explains the great
difference between Protection and Freetrade.
The latter goes by no defined rule; it sticks on
duties anywhere and calls them a “ revenue
tariff,” and thus the members of a Freetrade
Legislature have a favourable opportunity—
of which they always avail themselves—to
pass the burden on to someone else.
Protection travels on the policy of
taking off all imposts which cannot aid
any local industry, and putting on duties
which promise to do so, and under a
tariff formed on true Protectionist prin-
ciples, the duty now levied on sugar would
be shifted on to jewellery, and the kerosene-
tax —which presses on the poor, for the rich
man uses gas or electricity—would be planted
on pianos, and the impost would be removed
from raw coffee and cocoa and charged to
carpets and carriages, and sago would come
in frePjWvhile the tariff would come down
heavily %x imported furniture, marble, and
silver plate. Protection takes no note of
whether it levies upon the poor or the rich
or both, but in pursuit of its unvarying
principle it would make a woeful smash of
the privileges hitherto enjoyed by the rich
Foreign - traders of New South Wales,
whereby they shunt 99 per cent of the taxes
upon the labouring class, and call it liberty.
# # #
All this brings us back once more to our
starting point. Protection is not, as the
Foreign-trade press perpetually asserts, a
mere scheme for putting on Customs-duties,
for it takes off almost as much as it puts on.
And Freetrade is not, as its deceptive title
would imply, and as its mercenary advocates
declare, a policy whereby duties are taken
off, for it puts on as many taxes as it r e
moves. The incidence of taxation is shifted
but the taxation is there all the time. The
experience of Victoria has proved that a 25 p er
cent protective tariff costs the nation almost
exactly the same as does the present Freetrade
tariff of New South Wales. Therefore, when
the morning papers of Sydney rave about
the duties which will—in their favourite
phrase—be “passed on to the consumer,” it
is only necessary to answer that the amount
to be passed on to that unhappy and much-
advertised consumer bids fair to be just the
same amount which is passed on to him now
And when the grey-headed, toothless wheeze
about the “burden of taxation” comes
again to the front, it is worth remembering
that the burden is there already, that it has
always been there, and that even if Free-
trade were to last for ever the same old
burden would be there to the end of time. For
the “ load ” which has roused the bile of so
many dreary publications is simply the
amount paid by the nation at the Customs-
house, and when Freetrade and Protection
charge the same amount there is nothing to
shriek about. Meanwhile Protection under-
takes to make the old burden profitable, and
as tlie experiment costs nothing, and as Free-
trade can only guarantee to make the incubus
of taxation the same useless dead-weight as
before, it is at least worth while to make the
attempt. Even if the scheme were the in-
evitable failure which the Herald and the
Daily Telegraph foretell, it is better to fail
in the effort to do something than only to
succeed in doing nothing at all.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Another “Bank.”
The Northumberland Banking Company, which
recently went the way of all New South Wales
monetary flesh, is now going through the usual
process of the Sydney law-courts. It was a con-
cern which did a large business—and had at the
outset £73 10s. of available capital. Most of the
founders of the institution, it would appear,
paid up their shares by opening a current
account at the “bank,” paying nothing what-
ever into the account, and giving a cheque upon
it for the amount of their calls ! Very frequently,
accordingto the evidence, when a£so call became
due, the authorities went down to the office,
borrowed £75 without security, paid the call
out of it, and backed a horse with the differ-
ence, and consequently the more capital that
was paid the more desperately hard-up becanje
the Northumberland Banking Company. When
the employes wanted money, which they seem
to have done incessantly, they took it promis-
cuously out of the till, and when things reached
that point at which the ordinary banker’s clerk
would be run into gaol, this bank simply put
down the steals as “ officers’ advance account.”
When this account grew altogether too imposing
the plunder was sometimes put down as “sus-
pense account,” by way of variety. The
managing director seems to have applied to
himself for an overdraft without security, and
after duly considering his own unblemished
character and undoubted solvency, he gave
himself written authority to draw up to £5OO.
The managing director’s children both borrowed
money without security from the company,
and took up shares with it. The “general
manager ” paid £l5 on his shares, and borrowed
£7l 10s. to help him do it. The officers
furnished their houses out of the bank’s assets,
and some of them bought buggies and horses,
and paid for them with the deposits. Some of
the cheques drawn by one of the directors were
payable to “ Adam Bede ” (a sweep-promoter),
and a lot more were payable to various race-
horses, from which it is surmised that a good
deal of the funds went in gambling larks. The
simple rule of the Northumberland Banking
Co. seems to have been that whenever an officer
received a deposit he rushed out and “blewed”
it in clothes, or champagne, or jewellery, or
anything. Like the sparrows, the bank took no
thought for the morrow, and when it broke
there was about £9O on hand altogether, divided
among one head office and five branches. The
saddest branch of the lot had got through every-
thing except 3s. 2d., and the total amount
owing to depositors was £17,119 15s. fid. Also,
there was £1 to the company’s credit in the
Union Bank. Braund, a combination clerk
and auditor, who might have thrown some
further light on these matters, was found
drowned the other evening, but whether he
came to grief by accident, or whether he jumped
in to avoid cross-examination, or whether one
of his friends shoved him in to smother his
evidence, doesn’t appear. There never were
any directors’ meetings at the Northumberland
Banking Co. The managing director ran the
entire business in his own riotous fashion;
but other people apparently used to drop
in and empty the till now and then, and
therefore they may naturally desire to keep
things dark. Among the later revelations a
witness has been found who used to charge his
groceries and his draper’s and wine-merchant’s
bills to the bank, and other interesting items
are coming out daily. So far—strangely
enough —nobody has been put into gaol over
the business, and no depositor has shot a
director in a dark corner, nor has any enraged
mob hanged the manager on a telegraph-post.
Possibly, however, the creditors are only lying
low. The history of the Northumberland
Banking Co., as set forth in the daily news-
paper reports, is far stranger than aiiything in
the history of Mr. Montague Tigg.
♦
The Case of Edward Bell.
A few weeks ago The Bulletin published
sundry comments on the case of Edward Bell,
recently sentenced in Melbourne to four years’
imprisonment for burglary, and expressed its
opinion that the conviction was an almost
unparalleled instance of judicial stupidity. The
evidence against Bell was as follows: A


certain householder, named Larck, was
awakened by a burglar, and after a desperate
scrimmage in a dark room he wrested a coat-
tail off the intruder’s clothes, just as that
unlawful person fled through the window.
The only two things—judging by his report to
the police —which he was sure about in the
darkness and the excitement were the aforesaid
coat-tail, and the fact that the visitor was a
man of great size and ferocity. The police
thereupon arrested Bell—a miserably small,
stunted, undersized individual, as little like
LabCK’s description as a horse is like a dog —
the principal evidence against him being tliat
lie wore a pair of pants corresponding in
material to the captured coat-tail. He was
exhibited to the prosecutor —that is to say, the
latter individual wasn’t asked to pick him out
of, say, a dozen prisoners, but was simply
asked if Bell was the man ; and be promptly
identified him, just as he would, most likely,
have identified Long M‘Kean or “General
Mite ” if his attention had been drawn in the
same way to either of them. Also, he swore to
him in the Court, regardless of the awful dis-
crepancy between the size of the prisoner, and
that of the burglar as revealed by his own
description. Bell’s only defence was that the in-
criminating pants were given to him by some per-
son whom he couldn’t trace —being locked up in
gaol he couldn’t naturally trace anybody ; and
the jury promptly found him guilty, and the
Judge —regardless of Mr, Larck’s really sur-
prising statements —sent him up for four years.
This event took place some four months
ago, and within the past few days the real
offender —one Giles, who is now doing 14
years on his own account—confessed to the
crime. Giles and an accomplice gave the
pants to Bell, and as a further stretch of
humour they took the detective along, and
pointed Bell out to that functionary. The
officer, in fact, seems to have arrested his
victim simply because a shady-lookiug stranger
told him to do it, and apparently lie never even
asked how the stranger came by bis informa-
tion. Giles and his friend and supporter have
now been convicted of conspiracy to charge Bell
with the crime of burglary, but, so far, nothing
seems to have happened to Mr. Larck, whose
fluent and copious testimony certainly had
much to do with that miserable victim’s dis-
aster. No doubt if the burglar had committed
murder upon some member of the household to
facilitate his escape, Mr. Larck would have
sworn just as fluently, and long before this time
Bell would have been banged and forgotten.
Then, iu all probability, the truth would never
have been revealed, for no one worries much
about a dead man, and he would have gone
the same way as many other dead men who
fell victhiis to police stupidity, and circum-
stantial evidence, and the readiness of many
witnesses to take a prisoner’s guilt for
granted merely because he is in custody. And
even if the truth had come out iu that case, it
would have done remarkably little good to any-
body concerned. There is no return road from
the gallows, and hanging is the only mistake
for which the law can make no possible repara-
tion. And for that reason capital punishment
badly wants to be abolished. So long as the
law is a blundering institution, which depends
for its accuracy on the brains of unintelligent
policemen, who depend, in their turn, on hazy
witnesses and shady-looking strangers who
appear mysteriously at street corners, it is an
unspeakably rash thing for any court to commit
itself to an act from which there is no going
back. By pure accident, however, Bell has
been “pardoned ” instead of hanged. In other
words, the law, which did him a brutal wrong,
graciously forgives him for having the wrong
done to him. It is an unspeakable proceeding,
and the only thing the State can do now to
make it complete is to charge Bell for board
and lodging during the time be was in gaol, and
for wear and tear of the policeman who ran him
in, and for the use of the Judge who sentenced
him, and to send him a bill for the rent of the
witnesses who swore so profusely against him.
Lynch Law and Other Matters.
“Vulcan ” writes to The Bulletin as follows;
I have been a constant reader of The Bulletin for years,
and have carefully read your articles re capital punish-
ment, but must say my observation and experiences,
reaching from the year 1837 till the present date, in Tas-
mania, New South Wales, Victoria and California, do not
lead me to endorse your views. My firm belief is that as
long as such human wolves and monsters as Lynch,
Knatchbull, Sullivan, Vigers, Burdett, Lester, or Nicholls,
are barn, society, as the majority, is justified in depriving
them of the pleasure of committing a second or third
murder. You propose to confine such monsters for life.
How many valuable lives have been sacrificed to tliat idea
or whim? It is not long since a murderer in New Cale-
donia made his escape, and the Kanaka police sent to
arrest him had to drive a spear through him to do so.
That monster had committed seven murders, and had
been condemned to death four times ; and wdiat can be
expected of suoli scoundrels but that they will be con-
tinually plotting and scheming to regain their liberty,
not caring how' many more murders they commit in doing
so, because they know that they cannot lie awarded any
additional punishment, unless it took the shape of
tortur*, and I am sure you wouldn’t countenance
that. I am quite sure tliat if the abolition of
capital punishment party will give security that their pets
will never make their escape and commit a second
murder, that those who are now in favour of the rope
will cheerfully hand all homicides over to them to deal
with. I know' from personal experience and observations
that Human Wolves are born as regularly as human
sheep, and that the only effectual way to protect the
sheep is to kill the wolves. You talk about the brualising
effects of capital punishment. How could it brutalise if
it were carried out strictly in private? My opinion is
that the public trial and the reports and comments on the
press have a far worse effect than the execution, which
the general public never see. Do you believe that the
citizens of the great republic, who sometimes assist in
lynching a murderer, are more brutal than any
place where the British flag flies, and where such
things never occur, but where what is called the
lower orders amuse themselves by kicking women to
death witli hobnailed boots, fighting vicious asses for
gallons of beer, or backing themselves to kill mo.e rats in
a pit than a terrier dog, although very few of them ever
saw a man executed ? You seem to think that crime is a
hydra sort of affair, and tliat it is not advisable to cut
oil the head ot a murderer as it only encourages otlieis
to become murderers. Well, I never knew of acase where
after a man was hanged for murder half-a-dozen
others went and committed murder so that , they
wight he hanged likewise. But I do know that in the
early days of the diggings in California great numbers of
scoundrels wentjfrom the Austialian colonies to California,
which they couldn't have done if they had been hanyed at
home, and the citizens of San Francisco, in self-defence,
found it necessary to form a vigilance committee, with
the result that some of the scoundrels were hanged and
others were notified to quit, and the honest and quiet
Australian colonists were never afraid of tlie vigilants or
Judge Lynch., I also know that in those early days the
wiuers could leave their gold and other belongings in
their tents without fear, it being known that thieving
meant death. I also know that in the early days of the
rush to Bendigo and Forest Creek, with all the circum-
stances just the same as in California, that the diggers
used to take their puddling tubs, windlasses, and ropes (o
their tents at night to prevent them being stolen, as the
Victorian thieves had no fear of Judge Lynch or any other
judge. With regard to lynching, it is my opinion that it
is as necessary as vaccination, quarantine, or revolution,
to stamp out crime when the regular laws of the country
fail to do so, as they have often done in the past, and no
doubt will in the future. I challenge anybody to prove
that there lias been more miscarriages of justice under
Lynch law than under the regular laws of any country
in the world. I myself am sure that there have not
been one-twentieth as many. I understand you to
say that crime is the effect, and bad laws or vicious en-
vironments are the cause. 1 maintain that circumstances
never made a good man bad, or a bad man good,
I think you will admit that the people of Ireland have
been cursed with had laws for five or six hundred
years. Now, are the Irish more criminally inclined
in their native country than when they emigrate to other
countries, where they are under good laws and have more
liberty ? My experience is quite the contrary.
There are just about tour points in this letter
that are worth answering. The writer seems
to assume, in the first place, that all men who
are hanged are such as Lynch, Knatchbull,
Nicholls. and a few more, and that The
Bulletin desires the abolition of capital punish-
ment expressly to save Lynch, Knatch-
bull, and Co. from the gallows. The
individual who assumes things so recklessly
as that is surely unable to read print. The
few exceptional monsters he quotes are only
about 1 per cent, of those who reach the
gallows, and—so far as can be judged from all
available evidence—for every time the law hangs
a man like Knatchbull, it also hangs about
three innocent men by mistake. It is chiefly
for the sake of the innocent men whom the law
murders in its blundering efforts to find
Knatchbull that The Bulletin wants to see
capital-punishment abolished, for execution is
the one legal blunder which can never be
atoned for when the truth comes to light.
Secondly, the writer asserts that the Australian
criminals who went to California couldn’t have
gone there if they had been hanged before they
started. This is obviously true; also, the
embezzler couldn’t have embezzled if be had
been hanged before lie started embezzling, and
the drunkard couldn’t have drunk if he had
been executed before ho commenced drinking,
and it is obviously necessary, on this basis,
to bang everybody. Thirdly, he asserts
that lynch - law leads to no more mis-
carriages of justice than docs the ordi-
nary' process of the judge and the hangman.
Perhaps it doesn’t, for there is no reliable
record in either case. Lastly, he states
that “ circumstances never made a good
man bad or a bad man good.” Perhaps
not. Religion, civilisation, education, law—
lynch and other varieties —are all circumstances,
and if they make no difference, then let them
all be abolished. —Ed. B.]

□ell the State Hallways? Never!
Whenever New South Wales is itiore than
usually hard-up—which is a matter of frequent
occurrence—the question of selling the railways
invariably comes to the front, and there is much
talk concerning a certain vague and unspecified
syndicate, which would gladly take over these
properties at a cash valuation of £40,000,000.
The huge deficiency which Parkes left behind
him has again brought this matter to light, and
one of the Sydney Sunday papers publishes a
massive array of figures, showing the benefits
which would ensue when a crowd of foreign
speculators took over the principal asset of the
State. At the present moment, New South Wales
is tottering under a burden of debt, and if the
syndicate really exists, and if its £40,000,000 is
veritable cash, and not a mere visionary pile of
gold on fixed deposit in the clouds, the sale
would wipe out about four-fifths of the incubus
iii one act. The saving to the Treasury iu
interest would be enormous; the problem
of bow new loans are to be raised to repay the
old ones, which are now beginning to fall due
with awful regularity, would be got rid of; the
nightmare of the annual railway deficit would
bo removed ; and in a general way the
country would breathe again. This is the
case for the syndicate, and it looks remark-
ably well. But there is another side to the
question. The syndicate would borrow its
£40,000,000 in England, just as the New South
Wales Government has done, most of it,
probably, on debentures; therefore, New
South Wales would have to pay the interest to
the English debenture-holders just as it docs
now, and the load wouldn’t be shifted one soli-
tary inch. Under one system the Government
has to raise the money, somehow, from the
people of New South Wales; under the other,
the syndicate would have to raise it, also from
the people of New South Wales. The people
must pay in either case. If the syndicate suc-
ceeded in abolishing the annual deficiency, it
must do it either by increasing the rates—at the
expense of the people, or by reducing wages
and importing cheap labour —likewise at the
expense of the people ; the people defray the
annual shortage now, and they would do it then
also. The nation pays 3i or 4 per cent, on the cost
of construction at present; but as the syn-
dicate would have a monopoly iu all the
more thickly peopled parts of the province,
and its charges would not be limited by
competition, it would probably work things
up till it extracted from the country divi-
dends of 8 or 10 per cent.—in other words, the
country would pay twice as much as it does
now, and its burden would ultimately be
doubled instead of relieved. The syndicate
would not go in for railway extension except
where population was comparatively thick ; if
lines were wanted for the purpose of “ opening
up the country ” they would have to be made
by Government as heretofore, whereby the
State would presently own the unprofitable
railways, and the profitable ones would
belong to the great monopoly. The change,
at the very best, would be a change
of names—not of facts. If a great Rail-
way Trust were formed, and £40,000,000 or
so of the national debt were transformed into
special railway debentures, and the Treasurer
simply dropped the railway accounts and the
railway debt out of Ids financial statement
altogether, and the Trust were authorised to
raise rates and reduce wages to any extent, and
knocjc off deadheads, and do anything under
the sun so long as it paid its way —the effect
for the present would be just as good. For
the future, however, it would be better, for
when the Trust began to realise 8 per cent pro-
fits iu some far-off day to come, these profits
would go back to the Treasury, instead of
going to private Capitalists. And, meanwhile,
the national debt would be reduced in one act
to £10,000,0 )0, and if the Railway Trust still
owed the other £40,000,000 to the British
money-lender it wouldn’t matter —the syndicate
would have owed it in like maimer, and botli
ways New South Wales must pay the bill.
Also the members of the Trust could be paid
by a percentage on the profits—just as the
syndicate would be paid, and with this incen-
tive, and unlimited powers, they would
assuredly make the railways pay, or die iu the
attempt. Even a syndicate couldn’t do more
than that.
The cause of David Syme’s attacks upon The
Bulletin is wounded envy. His amour propre
has been stabbed by the celebrity—call it
“ notoriety ” or “ infamy ” and we are equally
pleased—which The Bulletin has attained in
England and elsewhere. Syme has read in his
own paper that the «Age has a circulation of
100,000 a day, but even Scotchmen do not live
by bread alone. The Age’s circulation is all in
the little Cabbage-garden,- and so is nineteen-
twentieths of the ponderous Leader’s; “The
Bulletin is everywhere, and everywhere it is
influential,” writes its critic, Christie Murray,
in the Contemporary Review. The see saw
Age has been at work for 30 years, and came
into David Syme’s hand ready-made by the
brain of his dead brother, Ebenezer. The
Republican Bulletin has sprung up within
eleven years, and is now better-known all
over the world than any other Australian
paper. The Age is only known within the
boundaries of its own little cockatoo-squattage.
It has turned its back on Radicalism and prints
leaders extolling Kingdom and Quecndom as
against Republicanism, the Australian destiny.
The nerve-centres of David Syme, like those of
all the proprietors of soi-disant “ groat ” Aus-
tralian daily newspapers, are in England, not in
the country which has, at least metaphorically,
converted them from living skeletons into
ham-fat men. Nobody in England notices
Syme and liis publications, which never by
any chance print any original matter that
travels the rounds of the world’s press. Hence
Syme squeals.

The Bulletin hereby calls upon the N.S. W.
Foreign-trade party to exhibit the courage of
its convictions, and to vote solidly against Mr.
Sheldon’s Bill prohibiting the indenting of
cheap aliens to swamp the labour-market ot the
province. The objection to these aliens is that
they reduce wages and increase competition,
but it is only the produce of their labour that
increases competition, and whether they grind
out that produce on the spot, or whether
they simply send it along per cargo-
steamer, the I’esult is the same—it gets
here iu either case, and it is the fact
of its getting here that causes the trouble.
Mr. Sheldon’s Bill is Protection of the most
unvarnished description, and if the Foreign-
trade party doesn’t oppose it, and if the Daily
Telegraph doesn’t write a series of frantic
leaders against it, then they are basely desert-
ing their principles in the hope of catching the
Labour-vote. We are waiting anxiously for
the Telegraph to shriek on this point, but up
to the time of writing it has either meanly
neglected the whole matter or else it has
shrieked in a whisper that was quite inaudible.
The Melbourne Gas Co. rulers have reduced
the wages of stokers, firemen, and engine-
drivers by sixpence and a shilling per day, “ on
account of the contemptible meanness of house-
holders in reducing their meter consumption.”
At the present moment the directors couldn’t
feel sure of paying a 20 per cent dividend for
the half year unless they knocked 0 to 10 per
cent off the moneys of their hardest-worked
employes. Secretary Vasey, by the way, talks
about “ the prevailing general depression ” as a
calamity which must necessarily affect every-
body except his bloated shareholders in a
monopoly which is no less sinful, though better
managed, than that under which Sydney groans
and grumbles. Depression in gas consumption
will gradually increase as the electric-light
comes along, and the Melbourne public will
find the price, per thousand feet, going gradu-
ally up, whilst the price of stokers works slowly
downward. But shareholders will continue to
draw their time-honored dividends, somehow,
until a “ distinguished visitor ” stumbles across
the gas scandal, and explains in a London
magazine article that Australians submit to
impositions that would stir the downtrodden
Cockney to rebellion.

Mathias Larkin, the fashionable Melboiune
swindler, was interviewed by an Age reporter
just after his moral bubble burst. The remarks
made by Mr. Larkin on this occasion need to
be read thoughtfully through the nose iu a
monotone, and along with a humble snuffle, to
bring out their full flavour. Here are a few
samples:—
The amount of my deficiencies will not amount to more
than £lO,OOO. I cannot exactly say where the money has
gone to. As far as £3OOO or £4OOO is concerned, I might
just as well have thrown it into the Bay for ail the real
benefit 1 received from it. Can’t say what I did with it.
I have made my bed, so must I lay on it. Several of my
friends are at work to effect an arrangement, but no one
has any authority to act on my behalf. A gentleman
came here to-day and offered mo £7500. He and I are in
partnership in a certain business in which 1 have a similar
amount sunk. At the office of the society to-day three
offers of £lOOO, £4OOO, and £3OOO were made, and as much
more would be forthcoming if it were required. Mr.
Skinner, the caterer, will also assist me to the extent of
£3OOO. Even the poor people are treating me with the
most considerate kindness. No, my land speculations did
not prove particularly bad. The face value of tire land I
hold is more than ample to requite all my obligations.
The Yarra Bank Land Company owes me £2OO, and I
have £OOO to draw from the South Wharfage Company.
The Suburban Land Company has to give me a cheque
for £l5O. being interest I paid on its account to the bank
last balancing day. This undertaking is in the bands of
a private syndicate, of which I am a member. It lias a
lot of valuable property which will be realisable some
day. Yes, I have been very foolish, but the bast of us
will trip sometimes. The papers, with onq exception,
hive treated me fairly and kindly, and I am glad I have
ha 1 the oppoitunity of unburthening my overtaxed
fellings. Good day, sir, and thank you.
This awful specimen of smug complacency can’t
be appreciated all at once—it needs to be
allowed to soak in. What Brother Larkin
frankly admits is that he is a rich man who
stole poor people’s savings from sheer greed and
dog-meanness. The papers, he says, treated
him “fairly;” that is to say, they let him
down very, very softly, and not one of
them mentioned what an irredeemable
snuffling hypocrite he was, but merelv
alluded to him as a gentleman struggling
under the pressure of misfortune. As he calls
this treating him “ fairly,” he evidently thinks
that the description fits exactly. But the apex
of Larkin’s cheek—the great pinnacle of gall
that rises like a cat hedral tower out of the dead
waste of his soul-blasting effrontery—is his
remark: “I have been very foolish, but the
best of us will trip sometimes.” If it costs
£lO,OOO when the fatted Mathias only trips,
he would be a dreadfully expensive man
on the occasion of a complete fall ;
and if he is “ foolish ” when he prigs £lO,OOO,
how much would he prig if he were a
complete idiot ? Why doesn’t he put the
situation in plain English, and say: “The
meanest skunk will be found out now and then?”
The promise of an inquiry into the
attaching to a certain Australian Judge is
scarcely sufficient to satisfy public sentiment.
We have unfortunate experience of depait-
mental inquiries, and nothing less than an
investigation by a select committee will serve
to allay the alarm and suspicion which have
been engendered by public imputations of
insolvency and gross misconduct. A member
of one of the labor organisations has brought
the matter in question prominently forward,
and improved considerably upon the allega-
tions which have already appeared in print.
The venerable gentleman indicated cannot
afford to affect disdain or ignorance in regard
to what is published and said respecting
his actions and character. A great statesman
may shamelessly exhibit his seamy exterior,
but a Judge cannot and must not. He is paid
not alone for the exercise of the “cunning,
wisdom, and discretion ” specified in his com-
mission, but for “ grace to execute justice and
to maintain truth.” Prayers are offered up
each week for the preservation of his integrity
and rectitude, and jf a man so distinguished
from his fellows can be proved to live the
reverse of a wholesome and exemplary life, ho
should be not retired but dismissed the public
service. Neither roue nor kite-flyer is entitled
to draw pension from the exchequer.
The insolence of Judge Harding (a unm who
has many times, between the dates of the
Swanwick contempt of court incident and the
recent shearers’ trial, shown himself utterly
unfit to act in even the meanest judicial capa-
city) is again to the fore. A email boy of 13
years was called before him at Brisbane the
other day, and said, in reply to the foolish
queries usually asked of juvenile witnesses,
that he didn’t go to Sunday-school, and that he
didn’t know there was a God —a question which,
if submitted in its Sunday-school sense to a
bench of sound logicians unbiassed by precon-
ceived opinions, would, we venture to say,
occasion much argument. “What arc you?”
asked the Judge. “I am a Freethinker,”
replied the small boy. The Court laughed, but
there was really nothing to laugh at, for the
small boy only said what he had been taught
by his parents to say, just as any Protestant or
R.C. small boy would have done ;
Mr. Mansfield suggested tliat a solution of the difficulty
might be found in the new Oaths Act, a copy of which
was soon procured. If is Honor, after perusing its pro-
visions, found that it did not apply to a case such as this.
It only dealt with persons having a religious belief. After
further consideration his Honor suddenly remarked, “I
can treat him as a blackfellow.” The hoy's countenance
fell painfully. “Or under the Kanaka Act,” suggested
Mr. Mansfield. His Honor then asked the lad if lie
understood that in case lie did not speak the truth he
would be punished. The answer being in the affirmative,
the boy’s evidence was taken
despite Judge Harding’s brutal and gratuitous
insult. The legislatures of all civilised countries
having agreed to respect the affirmations of
persons having no religious belief, it is not
for this overpaid man Harding to flout the
principle as embodied in the laws of the asinine
country which has seated him upon its bench.
George Black, M.L.A., who has lately been
writing, in Sydney Evening News, a series of
jaundiced articles on Melbourne, as viewed
from a strictly Foreign-trade standpoint, winds
up by committing himself to the Single-tax
theory in these words ;
On the other hand, there can he no absolutely freetrade
until Custom Houses arc abolished. Piior to the accom-
plishment of this radical change, the goal of the true
freetrader, some other method of collecting revenue must
be substituted. I know of no better, as social conditions
arc at present, than the resumption into the hands of
their creators-the entire community—of unimproved
land values Iw taxation. This attained, much will he
accomplished, nevertheless reform will not here find its
goal, but only its starting point.
This is tt noble theory, but The Bulletin
respectfully asks Mr. Black, as it asks all
Single-taxers, to have the courage of bis con-
victions, and preach confiscation openly. Like
every other Single-taxer, he assumes that when-
ever the land is resumed the income arising
therefrom will pay all the expenses of Govern-
ment. Therefore he must mean remorseless
confiscation, for if the Government compensated
the present landholder it must borrow a gigantic
sum to do it, and the interest on that sum would
swallow up the rents, and leave the ordinary
expenses of the State to be met by the Customs
House as before. And, unluckily, confiscation
doesn’t only mean the despoiling of the big
capitalist who has other resources to fall back
upon ; it means that, the workman is to be
deprived of the little suburban allotment for
which he has already paid twice its value, and
the small farmer is to be plundered of his
selection, and there is to be general ruin
all round. The Single tax-cwm-Foreigu-trade
individual never dares to go into details about
how his millennium is to be created ; he only
talks about how it will be after it is created,
and publishes diatribes against other people
because they decline to carry out the policy
which he will not even formulate, and to
accept the odium which he won’t take himself.
Land Nationalisation and Protection or Single-
tax and Foreign-trade—there are the two alter-
natives, and the country must choose bet wee
them.

The “National” Conservative Union, repre*
senting the great, solemn, stupid Tory party in
Great Britain and Ireland, has been in congress
at Birmingham. It has decided unanimously
to encourage the formation of a Labour-party
in the House of Commons, which is a surprising
item for the ancient images to put upon their
programme—only as it happens it is merely put
there to catch votes, and doesn’t mean any-
thing. For the Union also decided against the
abolition of plural voting, so as to make quite
sure that no Labour-party will come into exis-
tence ; it resolved that land shouldn’t be taxed ;
it moved and seconded and carried a resolution
for the disqualification of illiterate voters, and
as these number many scores of thousands in
Great Britain this, in itself, will be a woeful
blow to any possible Labour-party ; and it
decided that the grand old Established Church
shall be kept up as before in Wales by seizing
the heretical farmers’ and workmen’s tables and
chairs and bedding, and selling them at auction
for the parson’s benefit. And as the Union,
with Salisbury at its head, has thus solemnly
resolved to do everything in its power to crush
out any new democratic party that might other-
wise be formed, it doesn’t seem to matter much
whether it has any theoretical yearnings after
democracy or not. It is the same bigoted in-
stitution, despite all its disguises, and though it
puts shiny boots on its cloven hoofs, and coils
up its scaly tail and hides it in its pocket,'its
identity glares out conspicuously all the time.

A common fallacy exists in the idea cherished
bv the pastoralists that the present fall of 3d. a
pound all round in the market price of wool
arises from the over-production caused by
the enormous yearly augmentation of “ Woolly
Kangaroos.” As a matter of fact, the in-
creased output of the Southern Hemisphere
is counterbalanced by the diminished returns
of the Northern wool - growing countries.
The cause must, therefore, be sought elsewhere.
Perhaps the nearest explanation is to be found
in the late mechanical improvements which
have enabled cloth-makers to produce more
cheaply than hitherto, and which, strangely
enough, react on the growers of the staple.
Next the large consumption by the masses of
shoddy and cotton goods, which latterly have
been turned out of hand so admirably finished
as to deceive the eyes of all but the most
experienced in woollens. Lastly, the heavy
percentage charged by English and Continental
broking, shipping and selling rings has contri-
buted towards lessening the prime values of
Australian wools. In spite of all these, this
class has no cause to complain seeing that, while
the value of sheep for mutton was, prior to the
frozen-chamber invention, about 25., the present
price averages four times that sum.

A barrister sends us the following clipping,
from the (London) Law Times of 11th October.
1891 :
The Recent Operation of tub Death Penalty.— The
official returns, in the annual “ Judicial Statistics,” show
that during the ten years—lB79 to 1888, inclusive—in
England and Wales, 672 persons were committed for trial
for “wilful murder’’(not including manslaughter or non-
capital homicides). Of these 299 were convicted and sen-
tenced to death, whilst 373 were either acquitted or found
insane (viz., 231 acquitted and 142 found insane). Of the
299 condemned to death nearly one-half, or 145, had their
sentences commuted, whilst 154 were executed. These
299 included 50 women, of whom 9 only were hanged.
Daring this decade the convictions resulting from all
cases of legal procedure (including summary convictions
and fines) averaged 79 per cent.; whilst the convictions
resulting from all criminal trials (indictable offences)
averaged 77 per cent.; whereas in capital cases the con-
victions only averaged 45 per cent., and the actual inflic-
tions of the punishment of death barely reached 23 per
cent. So that persons charged with murder have many
more chances of escaping conviction than any other class
of offenders. For nearly 15,090 known murders in the
United States in.the six years, 1881-1839 inclusive, only 558
persons, or less than 4 per cent, were legally executed,
while 975 were illegally “lynched.” Further, there were a
largo number of suicides, and probably very many unre-
ported murders. In the four States where the capital
penalty is abolished, conditions are stated by competent
authorities to be less unsatisfactory than elsewhere. The
lynchings nearly all take place in States which retain the
gallows. In spite of the utmost care, it is known that
even the strongest circumstantial evidence has sometimes
led to the conviction and death of absolutely innocent
persons. There is much reason to conclude that, for
example, Wiggins, hanged in Ldndon in 1867, Hayes and
Stone, of Durham, 1873, and two of the three men hanged
at Leicester in 1877, with various others recently executed
in foreign countries, were put to death by mistake. And
in 1890 circumstances transpired which afford reason to
fear that a man named Blagg was hanged by mistake for
another man, at Chester, 1855. The evidence against
Blagg was wholly circumstantial, and mainly turned on
the'discovery of a shoe near the body of the murdered
man. It now appears that on the night of the murder
another person had obtained and worn those shoes. Blagg
earnestly asserted his own innocence to the last. Some
persons have been saved, after sentence, as by a hair's
breath, and ultimately shown to be innocent, as in the
cases of Polizzioni, of London ; Ilabron, of Manchester,
1876 ; Alice Rhodes, of Peuge, 1877 ; W. Siddle, of Dur-
ham, 1884, Travis, of Cheshire, 1889, and many more.
During the past year further instructive instances of the
operation of the laws affecting murder have occurred.
■ Thus 8000 signatures to petitions were lately sent
to the Home Secretary praying for the release of
a Lincolnshire man who was convicted of killing
his wife and mother in 1863. He was sentenced
to be hanged, but was transported, and has always
protested his innocence. After 26 years’ imprison-
ment he has been pardoned. This year, also, in the Pun-
iaub, six men were convicted of murder, but shortly
afterwards the supposed victim was found to be still alive.
In April, 1890, a man in New York State was pardoned
after 16 years’ imprisonment for a murder in Burke
county, which another man recently confessed on his
death-bed to have committed. One of the men hanged
this year, in London, on circumstantial evidence, strongly
protested his innocence to the last, and there is a possi-
bility that his plea was justified. A Spaniard who has
been imprisoned since 1887, in France, on a charge of
murder, has lately been found innocent and liberated.
Such cases, with many others, together with the diffi-
culties connected with'hereditary madness, often lead to
very embarrassing interferences with the execution of the
law interferences which never occur, on a similar scale,
except in the case of the death sentence. Thus, during
the year, 135 petitions, with 7500 signatures, were sent to
the Home Office, urging a commutation of the sentence
for a murder at Kentish Town (on the ground of insanity).
In other recent instances, there have been similar
numerous memorials. But law ought to be of such a
nature as to be enforced with the hearty support ®f the
community, and especially so in relation to the repression
of murder, _
The recent Buttner case at Sydney, in which the
convict—sentenced to death for rape—was released
from prison and awarded a State gratuity on the
very day fixed for his execution, through a dis-
covery accidentally made, affords a startling in
stance of the fallibility of human tribunals. The
perjury of the prosecutrix in Luttner was
established by her own confession. Her virtue and
Buttner’s brutality seemed incontrovertibly proved
by the evidence of many (including medical)
witnesses; yet the Government at the very last
moment obtained absolute proof, apart altogether
from her own admissions, that she had for years
been a woman of the town.

816 Macquarie-street North, Sydney, Dec. 2,1891.
Aly Dear Moorabinda,—
Recent arrivals from “ Home ” say that Lady Jersey’s
cacaethes loquendi is quite a sudden attack, and are fairly
surprised at her gift of the gab and lightning talkee-
talkee. And, in support of their astonishment, an
English society weekly (which surely cannot have heard
of her ladyship’s fierce speeches in the last British political
campaign) gushes that her Broken Hill speech “proves
her title to rank as a charming speaker,” and hints that
“this gift of her ladyship’s must not be overlooked by her
friends when she returns to England,” &c. By the way,
every additional speech shows how much “ colonial ex-
perience ” Lady J. has picked up. You never now hear
her hoping “ that the public school children will always
look clean as she did on her maiden stump at Albury.
I begin to disbelieve all those dreary English novels
which follow the sorrows of the governess to the bitter
end—to discredit that damp and tearful range of litera-
ture which depicts the fragile orphan of the deceased
country curate being subjected to the sneers and jibes of
her heartless employers and pupils. Now, judging from
Australian vice-regal samples, titled peoples’ governesses
walk on velvet. They are treated quite like “daughters,’’
and when they get married their cold and cruel employers
give them valuable presents and a handsome breakfast>
and their arrogant pupils shed real tears, and the air is
thickened with compliments. And, on the other hand, if
they don’t marry, they are glad to stay with the family ;
they help to entertain all guests, and they personally
back up their employer-friend in every festivity' in which
she has a hand. The English governess (of fiction) is a
trampled-upon, badgered consumptive, who ultimately
gives up the ghost on a damp doorstep; the English
governess of Australian experience has ten ball-gowns in
the season and her breakfast in bed if she wants it. And >
apropos of teacheresses, it is remarked that since Lady
Carington went away the craze for dragging the instruc-
trice of your hopefuls with you to “ ball, rout, and round-
about” has died away, and Mrs. de Potts Point now
dispenses with the lady in her retinue.
Are women becoming more business-like? or, is it that
the toil-worn first cause of the matrimonial manage has
tired of handing his wages over to a frivolous pansy-
blossom to dissipate as she pleases, and now demands
account of how the money goes? Why I ask is, that I
was buying a reel of cotton or something in a rag-shop
the other day, and had paid my money and was going
away, when the beautiful young man behind the millinery
buffet asked me if I wouldn't wait for the bill? “There
isn’t any change, so why should 1?” was ray bewildered
query', and he made answer : “ Most ladies take their bills
home with them, madam, to see what they spend in the
y-ear.” Which wilted me, of course, but I plucked up
courage to ask if more married than single ladies kept
their purchase-tags (in the hope that the husbands were
mainly responsible for this feminine carefulness, after all),
but I could get no comfort from him. “ They nearly all
do, madam,” he replied sternly, and I retired covered with
confusion, but pleased to hear that somebody was try ing
to live within her income, anyhow. Women can’t be so
reckless with the cash as they are supposed to be if they
treasure up all their reproachful “ Dr.’s to ” for a whole
year.
But you can be too precise over adding up your expen-
diture. There is a Sydney housewife who puts down
every penny she pays out—not when she gets home, but
right on the bargain-spot. This thrifty habit is observ-
able in its full beauty when she is standing afternoon-tea
to a crony in one of the ladies’ cafcis. When the waitress
hands the tag, this careful stewardess remarks in a hollow
whisper, “One-and-six !” fumbles in her hand-bag for her
ivorine tablets, and jots down the mad extravagance
before settling at the counter. Meanwhile, the crony
feels as if she had eaten her entertainer out of pock :t.
After all there cannot be very much significance in a
name, or even in two names. An old lady died in Sydney
the other day at the full-fledged age of seventy odd, who
married a man named Verge w'hen her own name was
Graves. Graves-Verge ! And yet she lived nearly three
quarters of a century'.
One of the heiresses in a Jarndyce versus Jarndyce-like
suit perennially on the list at one of the Supreme Courtg
got married the other day to a fortunate young lawyer’s
clerk with whom she had only been acquainted for
two weeks. Somebody took her to task for her precipi-
tancy and was greatly astonished when the bride made
answer : “ Oh, yes ; I only knew Reginald a fortnight-
hut then, you see, I was a great friend of a girl who had
known him for ever so long ! ” Now, isn’t that an original
lettre de recommendation for a husband ? As a rule, the
girl who has been chummy with Reginald in his salad
days, religiously and deliberately gives him away, lock,
stock, and barrel, if she can get within ear-shot of his final
fiancee.
The Polo match on the N.S.W. Agricultural Society’s
ground, on 24th ult., was watched by a few' society folks
—some of the girls going on horseback, and looking so
trim that I couldn’t help marvelling why it is they don’t
figure in the Centennial Park, and make that ideal
cantering-ground blossom like a rose instead of being
given to the rangers and an eternal horseman on an
oyster-dappled grey charger. The Park is the only pro-
perly-turfed riding track in Australia, and if people
would only follow the Caringtonian foot-prints a very
pretty equestrian spectacle might be presented to admir-
ing visitors. Why they don’t make a local Rotten Row
of "the turfed, safe Centennial track is quite their own
secret. Perhaps they are waiting for Lady Jersey to
announce her intention of driving there one day in the
week!
The oddest thing observable on board the convict hulk,
Success, is that all the gruesome, convict-frocked wax
figures have blue eyes. Whether it is that their living
prototypes really had cerulean orbs, or that the wax
modeller only had blue beads in stock at the time of con-
struction, I cannot say; the fact, however, is somewhat
severe on the sapphire-sighted community. I found the
old, teak-wood relic very interesting on the day I shud-
dered through her, although sassiety doesn’t seem to find
it so. Sassiety stops away from the Success with the
unanimity of one person. On my day a number of weird
people had evidently come to appreciate the sufferings of
personal friends, and one old lady with a hair-net, crino-
line, and demoralised umbrella floated from cell to cell
like a bee from flower to flower, and acted as showman
without a catalogue to whomever fell in her snare. Her
speciality was a thorough knowledge of the way to put on
leg-irons, handcuffs, and refractory collars, and she had
male sight-seers pinioned all over the place by her fair
hands in various implements of torture —“to let ’em fee
just wot ’em pore biys had to suffer.”
It is a sad and painful fact that a large and bony
skeleton is about to overshadow the household of Mrs.
James White—widow of the Hon. James White. The
skeleton is all that remains of Chester, and Mrs. White
is having it mounted in order that the gallant horse’s
memory will be kept green. This is better than selling
favourites off as cabhorses in their sere and yellow leaf.
Viewing 11. M. Stanley perched high and dry in Sydney
Centenary Hall (with his lectern placed so as to hide his
speaking visage from the middle portion of the floor
audience) clothed in spotless linen and a shiny face, as if
he had just soaped it violently, one can’t repress a feeling
of sympathy for the ex-pirate. Although he wasn’t the
good little tin god to the savages that he pretends to have
been, still he has admittedly been accustomed to bound
through the wilderness in search of ivory (and Living-
stone) dressed principally in a '450 navy revolver and a
puggaree, and it was as sad to see him chained down to a
little red covered table with a decanter of water upon it
as it is to gaze upon the caged jackal at the Zoo. At bis
Sydney debut the house was mostly “ sassiety,” so far as
I could see, but the high pews and balustrades
shut out the view of one’s fellow-woman in the
re-christened York-street Wesleyan chapel, and I
just made out that a handful of men and women wore
evening-dress and improved the landscape, but the
majority did not. And here I must rise to enquire why
when a society woman sees fit to come forth after sunset
in walking costume, her lord and master, who accom-
panies her, still reveals the whole of his well-starched
shirt-front? Amongst those who came with bonnets on
were Lady'lnnes (with Sir George Innes and Mr. Innes)
in a dark walking-dress and flashes of jet in a little
capote ; Lady Salomons in a rich-looking visite and a
pink poppy in her bonnet ; the Misses Halliday in winter
jackets and hats ; Miss Salomons in a gingham frock ; and
doubtless many more only I didn’t see them. I ne v °r
turn my head in church.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

Another athlete’s opinion. Australian
Athletic Club, May 3, 1891. Marshall Bros.
Gentlemen —I can honestly testify to the tonic

Yours truly, Professor Lawrie, Gymnastic In-
structor.
The late Guerry de Lauret, of Goulburn,
N.S.W., rarely if ever could be induced to speak
his native language, De Lauret was one of the
gentlemen-in-waiting to Charles X., and was
exiled with that monarch. He was in his own
right a Marquis, though he never aired the title in
Australia, and belonged to a family which ranked
high in Brittany. His cousin, Abbe Dugueny,
was shot by the Communists in Paris after the
war 0f.’70.
A verbatim report of the arguments in the
bigamy appeal case of M‘Leod before the Privy
Council has just been received in Sydney. When
published it won’t please Mr. Justice Windeyer,
as the trend of the arguments all through was to
justify the position of those who, like The Bul-
letin, considered that no interpreta’ion of a local
Act could give a local Court power to deal with an
alleged criminal for an offence alleged to have
been committed in a foreign country. One
ingenious illustration of the law in this matter is a
reference to the appeal case as to the liability of
“ colonists ” to the operation of the Income Tax
Act. In the Divisional Court the Judges differed,
one holding that the Act did not authorise the ex-
actionof thetax in England on income, derived from
property held in victoria ; the other holding that
the words of the Act were of sufficient grasp for
the purpose. Upon appeal, the' income was held
exempt, and a portion of one of the judgments
was quoted in the argument in M‘Leod’s case as
material to the point involved. That judgment
refers to the argument of “International Comity,”
and suggests that when the words of a statute are
wide enough to include persons or subject matter,
also taxable in the jurisdiction of another power
or people, it will not beffield that the Parliament
of England contemplated anything so subversive
of “comity.” We should now like to hear Sir
Alfred Stephen’s opinion on the judgment.
When they released McLeod, they censured Sir
Alfred Stephen for hanging Black Peter and, at
least, one other man.

H. M. Stanley, the dreary man from the
Congo, commenced a brief season at the Centen-
ary Hall, Sydney, last Monday, with a profound
and solemn lecture on the mystery of Darkest
Africa. The one fact which sticks out conspicu-
ously at the Centenary Hall is that H. M.
Stanley is not a humorist. Great, gaunt, barren
ribs of solid fact protrude everywhere out of the
shifting waste of his entertainment. Bare, brown
knobs of information rise in dismal grandeur from
the dead-level of the show', and inaccessible pin-
nacles of hard erudition overshadow it in every
direction. The orator is a man of petrified stat-
istics, and each separate item of knowledge is
presented in a way that seems to suggest that he
overtook it after a hard run, and killed it with a
club, and kept the remains for future use. He is
nothing if not profoundly accurate. When one of
his comrades dies in the great, lonesome wilder-
ness, he utterly ruins the pathos of the scene by
flying off the handle to state that the place was
TUS feet—or thereabouts —above the level of the
sea. When he reached Uganda, and made a
triumphal entry into that joyous capital, he
states, with hideous precision, the whole list of
the presents that were fired at him by the reign-
ing monarch, right down to 111 ears of green
corn —one of them short-weight. Stanley signed
the way-bill for that last ear, we should imagine,
under protest. He is dreadfully particular about
the corn and the other items, and if he was in the
midst of a thrilling story an hour later, and sud-
denly remembered that he was oneear out, hewould
go right back and correct the discrepancy. At
one time he seems as if he is about to give a
glittering account of a native war on the shores of
the great lakes, but he forgets about the battle,
and tells the exact length, breadth, and
depth of the big, royal war-canoe instead. If
he is present at the great battle of Armageddon he
will lose the whole show, for he will be away in the
rear somewhere, calculating by geometry the exact
cubic contents of the ammunition, and will miss
the struggle altogether. There are hundreds of
awful bangs and shrieks and slams in his narra-
tive, which .are understood to be the names of
native tribes; and he drags his hearers up huge
mountains as they escape out of some place that
sounds like a bull-voiced demon pounding on a
tank, and rushes them down theother sideinto some
other place that sounds like a steam rooster crow-
ing in old Greek roots. Then with a cold, wooden
expression he tells you the exact latitude of these
places, and their height above the sea, and the
principal vegetables that grow there, and passes
on with the grim solemnity of an iron catalogue
talking by piece-work. His pathetic spots are
disfigured by horrible whizzing noises which are
supposed to be geographical designations, and
when he comes across a tearful tragedy he dries
it, and stuffs it, and puts a ticket on it, and hangs
it up in front of the audience like a ham, with the
inscription, “Pickled Tragedy, 2s. fid.”
# * *
Nevertheless, the explorer has his good
points. His hair is beautiful, for one thing.
He is probably sincere, and the pious references
which would sound so much like snuffle in other
lecturers are delivered in all good faith. Having
been a small god for many years in the wilderness
he lives on a moral pedestal, and even if he
regards the Deity only as the senior partner in
the firm there is no visible touch of cant in the
situation. He has evidently revisedhis lecture care-
fully, and remorselessly knocked out the big “I”
wherever it threatened to become too prominent.
And once or twice, in describing his fearsome
voyaere down the Congo, he nearly became inter-
esting. But he dodged the danger successfully
after all, and when he was finished the flight
down the great, dark river was as dry and emo-
tionless and deadly commonplace as a journey
up the Yarra. If Stanley had been an eye-
witness of the storming of Constantinople he
would have described it in his lecture as a dis-
agreement between the Greeks and Moslems in
latitude 30 deg. oast, longitude 40 deg. north, in the
course of which the Turks climbed over a wall 50
feet high and destroyed much property ; no insur-
ance. If this particular explorer means to do any
good business with his lecture ho will get Rider
i laggard to re-write it for him. If Rider Haggard
D too busy, or his terms are too exorbitant, w r e
will refix the oration ourselves at a reasonable
rate on the receipt of Smythe’s cheque. Then,
when the lecture is refixed, Stanley should hire an
elocutionist to deliver it for him, and simply
stand by during the operation as an evidence of
good faith. He is too hard and callous of voice,
and too gritty of delivery to make a success of the
job in person, and if he was as unsympathetic,
to his rear-guard as he is to his audienee it is no
wonder that unhappy institution went wrong.
We fled for home after the lecture, with the
speaker’s dismal"“tnonotone still in our ears, and
took Stanley with us into the land of dreams.
And then all through the night we were following
a big sheet iron explorer w’ho puffed along by
steam through dank forests, where baleful green
eyes glared among the trees, and up huge moun-
tains and down the other side, and over swamps
where the water got into the explorer and put his
fire out, so that we had to hunt for dry wood and
re-kindle him ; and then at last we found a wooden
Livingstone sitting on a ton of ivory and preach-
