“ Pickpocket ” has never been better
played than now at Sydney Criterion —in fact,
nothing but a theatrical drought prevents
the auditorium from being overflowed nightly.
Andrew, the rheumatic, bilious, shiny-clothed
waiter with the bulgy boots and the rusty
cough, is one of Dot Boucioault’s best parts.
Arthur Lawrence succeeds Harwood and Anson
a s Gregory Grumbledon, the robust valetudi-
narian, and runs him chiefly on the lines of the
former. On the whole, his performance is not a
great with that of either.
Titheradge, as Osmond Hewett, is, of course,
O.K. Mr. Lyndal’s too loving husband, Frederick
Hope, is over-fussy in the first act, but otherwise
all right in a thankless part. Clitherow is ex-
tremely funny as the wild lunatic, and G. L.
Gordon’s Dr. Shaw (a Shakesperian name, by the
way) is quietly effective. Other previous per-
formances, including Miss Romer’s Miss Trumper,
and Pattie Browne’s Freda, are up to old-time
excellence. Miss Spong’s little wife is about her
best effort. On Saturday next the Juvenile Comic
Opera Company open in “ Mikado.”
* *
The Government having decided that
Sells Bros*; horses shall remain in quarantine until
time’s up, and then be shipped back to America,
one of the brothers is now drilling an assortment
of local nags to fill the vacancy, and hopes soon to
have the hippodrome in all its glory once more.
“ The elements have conspired against” Sells so
far that Friday’s gale compelled them to stow the
canvas of the big tent and discontinue their per-
formances, but biz. is splendid, and the season has
been extended till December 9. Visiting the show
again, the Jorella Bros, on the trapeze and Mine.
Costello on the serial rings, recommended them-
selves to us as folks worth watching. Outside the
side-show is well worth a shilling; there may be
seen headless, legless, bustless, armless, trunkless
women, all young and beautiful, and sleight of
hand tricks by the lecturer, who is decidedly
amusing. Last week we inadvertently rechristened
the courteous Colonel —whose hind name is Seeley
—as “ Sellars.”
** * *
“Evangeline” is doing good gallery and
stalls’ business at Sydney Royal, and so far seems
to be received with infinitely more favour than
was “The Corsair,” by audiences who were never
disposed to swallow Fortescue in the lump unless
the “Man in the Moon” were added as leaven.
Two surprises were thrown in the other
evening. At the close of the first act a young
lady dropped a usually - accounted indispen-
sable portion of feminine apparel, and got so
paralysed by shame that she didn’t know enough
to kick it out of the way, and stood blushing
while the audience sniggered. Then George
Walton, who comes off tour to take the place of
Johnny Gourlay gone on tour, and who plays Le
Blanc more quietly, but on similar lines, after
hauling for some minutes on the hawser, to
whose extremity is usually appended a small dog,
lost his time for the packthread end came on
without the puppy. The singing of Messrs.
Edwards, Fitzgerald, Brown and tire, is still a
big feature of the show, and the Amazonian
march always snatches in the baldheads.
** * *
“Jo” is not so well played at Sydney
Garrick as we’ve previously seen it. Some good
people are unsuitably placed, and some indifferent
performers are placed over well. G. P. Carey
usually does everything so well that it affords“us
much gratification now to assert that his In-
spector Bucket, vice Burnett retired, wholly lacks
the official tone and air that pervaded it formerly.
Adelaide Cushman, late with Dan Barry, has
joined the Garrick to play Guster, and Frank
Hawthorne, formerly of a travelling company, is
misfitted as Chadband. Jennie Lee, as of old, is
the whole show and nothing but the show ; never-
theless, her voice seems too well greased with
good living to hold throughout its former raucous
huskiness, but her Jo is indisputably one of the
finest pieces of character acting known to the
English stage. Business is not over good. H. M.
Stanley and the other wild-beast show are doing
most of the houses such a heap of harm that things
theatrical are very much unsettled.
* * * ac-
Busincss at Her Majesty’s is, on the whole,
more consistently good than at most Sydney
places of amusement, proving the truth of the
adage that when “ Youth’s” at the prow, pleasure
must take the helm. Therefore, no change of
bill is imminent.

This (Thursday) afternoon, Alfred Wyburd
makes a large splash in the placid fish-tanks of
Bondi Aquarium. On this occasion “the finny
denizens of the deep ” (this phrase is copyright)
will be introduced to the Mayor and aldermen,
Superintendent Bear and other officials of the
fire-brigades, the British cricketers, among many
athletes, and several other wise, distinguished and
common plaice soles. The Theatre Royal orchestra
will perform ; several M.P.’s will speak, and
heaps of amusement will be provided.
** * *
Caterers for public amusement would do
well to follow the example of Manager Larmour,
whose engagement of the Misses Albu and the
Elite Quartette drew such an audience to Coogee
Aquarium last Sunday that the hall could scarcely
contain them. As a draw, half-a-dozen first-class
performers are worth a host of duffers. The Misses
Albu will be benefited at Coogee on Saturday
next, when all the local talent available will assist ;
and will appear again next Sunday.
** * *
George Darrell has respectfully submitted
“ Hue and Cry ” to the critics and playgoers at
Melbourne Opera House, but the world is wagging
on as usual. The hue of George’s latest work is
a pale, monotonous drab, and its cry never rises
to the importance of a howl, or the dignity of a
piercing shriek. In this instance, too, the pre-
siding genius, spreading himself over long lapses
of years, slowly mellows into middle age under
the disguise of a weird, inhuman dialect, and a
profusion of crimson hair. Were it not for his
dear familiar legs, trusty boots, and unalterable
tones from the far distance, one would have a
difficulty in swearing to George’s identity, especi-
ally at those times when he drops into a waist-
coat by mistake. Surrounded by a wild Irish
wife and a couple of full-grown “ childer ” (who
cling to t’ould fayther, notwithstanding all that
the wigmaker has done for him), he is indeed a
gruesome change from the limelight Adonis of
the old blood-and-thunder days. Unfortunately,
George’s laudable endeavours in the direction of
novelty begin and end with his get-up. He
is a miner as of yore, until he strikes it
rich, and retires into private life as a com-
monplace scarecrow with a warm ’art. A villain
in a silk hat still pursues him, whilst the
well-remembered new-ehum, a comic Chow, an
elderly “ baronet of the realm,” and other stock
Darrell i an accessories pop up much the same as
usual. After opening with a promising murder
the drama degenerates into a weary interchange
of platitudes, relieved by surprises as inevitable
as the water-rate. The one really astonishing
feature about “Hue and Cry” is that George
doesn’t finish up by finding a strawberry-mark on
the left arm of the realmy baronet, and claiming
that perennial idiot for his long-lost brother. In-
stead of which he merely produces the charred
remains of a letter, that have lain hidden in his
pocket for 28 years, whereby the baronet discovers
a niece in one of the alleged childer, whom
George has adopted, in the second scene,
with this end in view. Christine Darrell
takes more than a half share of the
“fat” in her husband’s latest endeavour on
behalf of the family, and is less like human nature
than ever. Her self-consciousness and gush are
positively distressing in one so young and good-
looking. Christine is generally more unreal and
bizarre than a Dresden china shepherdess, both
in manner and appearance. Her dressing in the
hour of adversity, when she wears beautiful light
brown stockings to harmonise with her costume,
is as extravagant as her acting, and she requires
toning down at all points. For the rest, Jenny
Bryce, Lillie Bryer, and Edwin Kelly are very
satisfactory, Greenaway passes muster as the
villain, and young Will Bernard makes some little
headway against his unhappy twang. Charles
Holloway’s twang is past praying for, but he and
one or two others bring experience to bear upon
the subject. Also the staging is satisfactory,
Longstaff’s picture, “Breaking the News,” being
effectively realised in a tableau.
* * * *
At the Royal there was a spasm of excite-
ment on Saturday night when a frenzied strangel-
- the refreshment contractor in one of hi s
braces, and the victim fell back into a bystander’s
arms under the impression that his end had come-
Nobody fired a cannon at “ Rob Roy,” however>
consequently that exhausted entertainment has
just died a natural death amidst gloomy surround-
ings. On Saturday, by the way, Walter Bentley
assumed the character of Nicol Jarvie (vice Cath-
cart) with dry and uninteresting results. He was
natural enough, but a natural Scotchman is not
necessarily a rich fund of amusement. Bentley
will now be required to fully account for his
presence in “ Hamlet.”
* * * *
“The Gondoliers ” has kept business brisk
at Melbourne Princess’, but the belief that it
might be made a stronger thing seems to gain
ground, and many people are content to await
future revivals of an opera which would certainly
prove itself grateful for better treatment. In the
meantime, also henceforth and forever, that long
scene between the Duke of Plaza-Toro’s daughter
and her ardent drum-major would stand cutting
an inch or two shorter. The references to “a
quarter of an hour ago ” became irritating at last,
and the performers, suspecting that W. S. Gil-
bert’s humour is off-colour for the nonce, evidently
feel their position keenly. Little Violet Varley
fell ill, or something, the other day—as damsels
will when the music don’t suit them —and Nina
Osborne has been singing in her stead, without
making much difference one way or the other. On
Saturday they revive “Marjorie”—which is a
gorgeous spectacle, anyway for that elastic
period “a limited number of nights.”
* * * *
“Dr, Bill” is finishing strong, as the
touts say, at Melbourne Bijou. Myra Kemble
now puts more dash into her share of the work
than she did some months ago. Her way of
beguiling Cecil Ward to take a cup of tea would
make a good many men swallow the spoon out of
sheer devotion. Further notes of admiration for
the company in general are unnecessary, because
“The Silver Shield” (first time in Melbourne)
goes up for six nights on Saturday.
* * * *
Dampier has taken the exact measure of
his public in “The Great City,” and the ruffians
who roam around Fitzroy and Collingwood, on
the look-out for innocent creatures that require
removal, are nightly hooted by fairly large
audiences. The treasury barometer, indeed, rose
steadily during last week, so it seems likely that
this lurid local narrative will remain popular till
nigh on to pantomime time. Dampier’s prepara-
tions for “Jack the Giant Killer,” in which Aggie
Kelton plays hero, are on a scale of splendour
hitherto unapproached at the “ Hallick,” and he
is advertising for new and shapely legs with wild
prodigality.
* * * *
There is to be a great German night at the
Alexandra, too, on Monday, December 7, when
Madame Wiedermann, Herren Hartung, Schmidt,
Rudolph Himmer, and other well-seasoned ama-
teurs will play “ The Marriage of Figaro,” out of
respect for the memory of Mozart. The same
company gave a really creditable performance of
serio-comic opera, some years ago, in the same
theatre, so it is reasonable to expect that Mozart
will not feel called upon to blush for the honour
heaped upon this anniversary of his decease.
* * * *
The Gaiety and Vic. still do their best to
upset “the prevailing depression.” Ditto with
the Waxworks, whilst the Gycloramas go on
scooping all the patronage which such noble
works of art and revelations in pictorial
are fairly entitled t®.
* * m *
Flabby London Figaro, commenting on
the Villiers fiasco with “The Dancing Girl ’ has
the patronising stupidity to “surmise that the
cold reception was due to the players and not to
the play, for the sake of the literary taste of our
Australian cousins.” Why, it was left tor Aus-
tralia to discover that the Duke of Gooseberry is
the sickliest, preachiest illustration of a rotten
hereditary system that ever dazzled the eyes of
the good old British snob. Also, that his lame
duck, whose mission is to put the Duke’s pieces
together and pull him from under the horses’
hoofs, is a nuisance on three sticks. London
critics can’t understand that they are apt to go
into hysterics Over a play which seems to sparkle
amidst the heap of rubbish they are called upon to
sift every month, whilst Australian audiences get
only the “home” successes to choose between, and
are seldom taken in by an article that has little
value outside the cockney market.

The seamy-side of circus life has been coming out in the
Melbourne papers, over affidavits about two little Warren
Bros., whose relatives want to destroy their glorious
prospects in connection with Wirth’s Wild West.
A Melbourne paper printed a gruesome portrait—taken
on the premises by a new imaginative process—of Lydia
Manton, in order to give its readers some idea of what
that “ beautiful Gaiety girl ” looked like before she com-
mitted suicide all on account of a prince. The artist
evidently went to Kreitmayer’s Waxworks for an idea of
the fair Lydia. Her wide expanse of chin and round,
aggressive eyes are incompatible with self-destruction,
but the next time a prince slits hiswizand after swallowing
a quart of prussic acid, Lydia will do nicely for a fancy
picture of the damsel who druv him to it.
Mrs. Bernard Beere has positively made up her mind to
go to Australia, and, reversing the order of things, work
back via America. Musgrove will run Mrs. Beere’s Aus-
tralian tour. He will have a good company to support
her. “By the bye,” remarks our London correspondent,
“ won’t you soon have more managers than actors?”
It is reported on bad authority that Stanley is going to
lecture somewhere on the dead niggers who marked the
track of his last expedition, the floggings and hangings,
the females—if any—who were abducted, the alleged
cannibalism of the rear-guard, and the howls and blood-
shed of the British major who was left behind with the
baggage and went raving mad in a swamp. The lecture
will be illustrated with views of the darkey who got 100
lashes, and an African connected with the expedition will
come on at the close of the lecture and state his unbiassed
opinion of Stanley in native dialect. A large attendance
is expected.
Duncan Ross, a correspondent tells us, parted with
Donald Dinnie at Cooktown and went North alone. At
Thursday Island a Rotumah darkey went on the stage
and equalled Duncan in all his lifting feats. Ross then
proceeded to Batavia and opened to a £l2O house. A
local swordsman gave him three prods and received only
two in return, so was declared winner of the 250 guilders
prize hung up by Ross. A wrestler came on the scene,
but Duncan could not put him down, so the Dutch audi-
ence became enraged and rushed the stage. About 300
infuriated Hollanders attacked him with sticks, when he
retreated outside and gave them the slip, leaving them
belting at each other in the darkness.

disputed trifles, appeared in person at the Melbourne

Mrs. Langtry denies that she is coming to Australia.
By the way, Mrs. L. is now completely and absolutely out
of society. Langtry won’t divorce her, and won’t give
her a chance to divorce him.
A leg item;—“There is a fascination about a pair of
shapely limbs which never palls. I am not philosopher
enough to explain it, but I am quick to feel its mysterious
power. But this is true of both sexes. Why, look at
Irving. He talks to you with his legs, or rather they
talk to you. They deliberate, hesitate, falter, coax,
threaten,'repulse, lie in wait, rejoice and are sad. Now,
I am willing to admit that a woman cannot do so much
with hers. They are more intractable; they have not
the purely intellectual and coldly analytical power which
a man’s have; but they have a power of their own, an
indefinable grace and charm of poise, which will till the
crack of doom make them one of the great attractions of
the mimic stage.”
A lady who lately visited Gounod says :—“ Sometimes
in the dead of night an inspiration would come to him.
He’d spring out of bed, light the gas, start to work, and
disturb the whole house. His pet whim was to lie abed
in the morning, and play cards with two pretty house-
maids seated at a small table by the bedside. He was
partial to a horrid red shirt, he often forgot to wash, but
his conversation enchanted the listener, and made one
oblivious of his outward self.”

bears this inscription—“ Rosalie Durand Lyster, died
8 Dec., 1866, aged 33 years.”

According to rumour, Musgrove is buying up no end of
plays, good, bad, and indifferent, in London, for Austra-
lia. I gave you a good tip (says our correspondent) when
I said it would not be long before he was back in Mel-
bourne. He has made a big hole in his cheque. Nellie is
very nice, but she will have to return without startling
the London playgoers.
Christie Murray (writes our correspondent) is working
like a nigger just now, and he has commissions for more
stuff than he can turn out. His earnings last month from
magazine and newspaper contributions alone amounted to
£260, and he will make even more this month. He anti-
cipates a very successful tour in the provinces with
“Ned’s Chum.” When he will start his venture is not yet
settled. The clever lad, Byrne, is meantime rusticating
in Lincolnshire with some relatives.
Farcical comedy (writes our London correspondent)
still holds its own, and there is no sign of waning popu-
larity, especially when the subject is a bit risque. The
latest, and, to my idea, the very funniest, of these farces
was produced last night at the Comedy. It is called
“ Godpapa,” and is the joint work of novel-writing F. C.
Phillips and Brookfield, the actor. Lucky the man who
purchases the colonial rights, for it is sure to boom, even
though you have no “champion Ijar” (on the stage) like
Charlie Hawtrey, or a merry minx like Lottie Venue.
“ Godpapa ” (originally passed the censor without com-
ment, but that functionary somehow began to think that
he had been somewhat lax, and he accordingly recalled
his licence, and the piece had to undergo considerable
howdlerisation. What it must have been in its original garb
I cannot say, but, judging from the tropical nature of the
revised version, it must have been a veritable scorcher.
Henry Arthur Jones, in the preface to his lately-pub-
lished drama, “Saints and Sinners,” says:—“lf it be
objected” (speaking of his descriptions of the modern
middle-class Englishman) “ that they are rather common-
place and uninteresting, I can only urge in defence that
it is impossible to suppose that God Himself can have
taken any great degree of pride in creating four-fifths of
the present inhabitants of the British Isles, and can
hardly be imagined as contemplating His image in the
person of the average British tradesman, without a sus-
picion that the mould was getting a bit out of shape.” In
the ordinary course of things this would be enough to
damn “ Saints and Sinners,” but as the play deals with the
adventures of an aristocratic seducer, J. Bull and his wife
forgive Jones’ blasphemy and rush off to see the fun.
When Melba first appeared in London, Augustus Harris
sat on her. She is now paying him back in his own coin.
Her terms are that she will never sing more than two
nights a week, and those fashionable nights. She insists
on being advertised six clear nights in advance. She is to
select the artists who appear with her. She demands
that the operas be properly rehearsed. She claims the
right to sing at private concerts for herself, and she will
not allow Sir A. Harris to put up cheap prices; and,
finally, she insists that the press shall be invited on every
first night of her performances.
Apropos of Lord Lytton, whose sudden decease last
week is cabled, the story of Victoria Cooke, Empress of
the Arena, will bear telling again. George Titheradge
was the histrionic elocutionist chosen by Lytton to read
the Imperial Proclamation, at the Delhi Durbar, where a
lot of gold medals were handed over to the native princes,
and some remained over, inscribed “Victoria, Empress of
India,” whereupon a bright idea seized Lytton. He took
a medal, scraped out part of the inscription, and changed
it into “ Victoria Empress of the Arena,” after which he
publicly handed over the medal to the girl Cooke, in John
Wilson’s circus ring, Calcutta. Consider the latent
sarcasm here, even on Jingo Lytton’s part, towards his
peerless Empress ! No wonder Yates, in the World— now
cabled to be very ill, by the way—got hold of a big sensa-
tion. Victoria Cooke afterwards visited Sydney and
Melbourne professionally.
Signor Agrati, now here with Sells’ Circus, has paid
altogether six visits to Australia.
Charley Arnold has been playing “ Hans the Boatman”
and “ Carl’s Folly ” in Ireland.
They say an actress nearly burst up the show, in
Sydney, by appearing on the stage when badly in the grip
of influenza. But we don’t believe it; actresses never
take it during business hours.
Myra Kemble just cleared expenses with a little bit
over by her Queensland tour. Charters Towers was the
most prosperous spot she struck ; there, she performed in
a skating-rink and sold standing-room, not seats, and the
spectacle of the entire population filing through the
streets, carrying chairs on their heads, is described as
being suggestive of a general exodus.
The Oharringtons and the faithful Fleming sailed for
India in the Arawatta.
J. Hamilton Magee, of Sydney Criterion, has come into
a small fortune by the death of his sister in Ireland. He
goes homeward to inherit, but returns to harness as soon
as possible.

Stanley mourning over his lost boat in the Congo :
“We hoisted it up on the rocks and left its bones to
bleach for she had nobly fulfilled her mission.” That was
where he came in, we presume.
This is a sample of the sort of alleged African legend
that Stanley fires into his audience : —“There was a lion
once that lived up in the forest. He was a very large and
strong animal, and he always went about saying ‘ I am the
strongest animal on earth.’ He used to walk up and down
challenging the other animals, but none of them would
ever fight him. But at last one day a rabbit, which had
got tired of the lion’s incessant boasting, came to him
and said: ‘Oh, Mr. Lion, I hear you are the strongest
animal on earth.’ ‘Yes, Mr. Rabbit,’ said the lion.
‘ Well,’ said the rabbit, ‘ I met a very strong animal out
in the woods to-day and he says he wants to meet you.’
‘ Well, Mr. Rabbit,’ said the lion, ‘ bring him along—
I’m his man.’ Then the rabbit took the lion to a
place in the woods where he and all the other rabbits
had enclosed a circle with very strong palisades,
and he said to the lion, ‘Go right in there—you will
find him waiting for you.’ When the lion went in he
closed the door and left him there. Then he called to
him, ‘ Oh, Mr. Lion, are you quite ready to meet that
champion,’ and the lion said,,‘ Yes, yes, hurry up. Bring
along the champion ’ Then the rabbit went away for 24
hours, and when he came back he called through the cage
again, ‘Oh, Mr. Lion, are you quite ready?’ And the
lion said, ‘Yes, of course, I am. Bring the champion
along and I’ll meet him.’ And the rabbit said, ‘ Just
wait a minute, and I’ll fetch him ; he’s coming.’ Then
the rabbit went away again for 24 hours. Next day he
came back, and by this time the lion was getting very-
hungry, and he said, ‘ Mr. Lion, are you ready to meet
that champion?’ And the lion said, ‘Yes, yes, I’m
getting tired of waiting. Bring him along.’ And
the rabbit went away for 24 hours more. And when
he came back the lion was so exhausted from hunger that
he could hardly lift a paw from the ground. And rhe
rabbit said ‘Are you there, Mr. Lion? Are you quite
ready?’ And a faint voice from inside said ‘Oh, yes;
make haste and bring along that champion, or I shall die
of famine.’ But the rabbit went away for 24 hours more,
and when he came back there was no voice inside, for the
lion was dead. And the rabbit said to himself: ‘ Ah, the
lion has found someone strong enough to meet him at
last. And the of that champion is Hunger.’ ” This
is a much-abbreviated copy of the anecdote, for the
original yarn took about ten minutes in delivery. The
moral probably is that a lion needs his victuals regularly,
and as a sort of subsidiary moral it is probably intended
to show that you shouldn’t chaff a starving lion in a cage.
Anyhow, it was the saddest, flabbiest anecdote we ever
heard, and when it was told slowly and monotonously the
effect was suicidal.
This is another of Stanley’s unduly exciting anecdotes :
“ When I was in U-gan-da an in-tell-i-gent lit-tle black
boy. a pa-age to the king, asked me, ‘ Why is it that all
the white men have long nos-es and all the white men’s
dogs have short nos-es, while all the black men have
short nos-es and all the black men’s dogs have long
nos-es?’ 1 needn't tell you the answer.” Then we held
ourselves down with both hands, else we would have
yelled out in despair: “ What was the answer?” Did
Stanley tell the small nigger that he didn’t know ? Did
he say that the dog’s nose was the act of God? Or did he
simply order the half-grown heathen to shut up ? Did he
stand drinks for the black infant, or tell him to “ ask me
something easy?” or did he give him a Bible and say in
hollow tones, “This is the secret of England’s Great-
ness! ” Evidently there is some answer to the conundrum
that is so stupid, so dull and blank and leaden and
ineffably dreary, that Stanley took it for granted it must
occur to everybody. But it didn’t occur to us all the
same, and we object to half-finished anecdotes.

returns to give two farewell lectures in Melbourne.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Now that Bray leaves to take the Agent-
Generalship, Coalition is the only thing to save
the Playford, (S.A.) Ministry.
Parkes has for the third time “ shut the
dour of his political life for ever,” and, as usual,
has bolted it firmly—with “ a boiled carrot.”
The reasons adduced in the N. S. Wales
Assembly for not enfranchising soldiers, apply
witli equal force to every other branch of the civil
service.
Jawbone Neild declares that the Orange-
men of N.S.W. control 25,000 votes and in-
directly influence 100,000. This is all very pretty
on paper.
Bechcr, erst of Grafton (N.S.W.) Grip,
now of Charlton (Vic.) Independent, is candidate
for the representation of Korong in coming Vic-
torian election.
It is pretty certain that the Protectionist
N.S.W. Ministry will beat the Foreign-trade
Opposition on the division after the ensuing
Budget debate.
Dr. Cockburn has been a member of the
S.A. Legislature for seven years, and in all that
time he lias only been absent for one sitting.
What a horrid grind !
Parkes is going down to Kiama ! He is
billed to lecture on Federation, but his motive,
doubtless, is to belittle Toby Barton for fathering
the “ holy cause” just a little too bumptiously.
Sir Alfred Stephen disputes the right of
wage-earners to exclusively style themselves “ the
people.” The State pensioner is as much one of
“ the people” as the hangman or the grave-digger.
The other day a N.S.W. Labour-member
got a letter from a racing club in his constituency
calling on him for a guinea subscription. Ap-
pended was this postscript: “Another general
election seems imminent— verb, sap.”
It is said that Premier Dibbs is agreeable
to summon R. A. Brice, as a Labour representa-
tive, to the Legislative Council. Is R.A.P. wait-
ing to see if H. H. Brown loses his seat for
Durham before he shows his hand ?
R. Caldwell M.P. (S.A.) has introduced a
Bill providing for the limitation of interest. The
maximum rate is fixed at 10 per cent. Under the
Bill agreements containing higher interest can-
not be registered and will not bind.
Amongst the members of the N.S.W.
democratic party, an earnest determination is
expressed to cut down the enormous military
estimates. Competent instructors and officers
could be got from Germany for half the money.

During Parnell's brief career, wages in
Ireland rose, on an average, 40 per cent, all round.
He brought the rents down, and the money that
was saved from the landlord went direct to the
labourer. Truly there is a tremendous power in
holy snuffle, when under its influence a whole
nation would round on the leader who had been a
benefactor to 4,000,000 human beings, merely
because lie had been rather less than a benefactor
to one woman.

Dear Bulletin, —Reading a par. in your
last issue anent the raising of rents in Newcastle
(Eng.), consequent on the passing of a Free Edu-
cation Bill, reminds me that when the coal-
lumpers and wharf-labourers of Sydney obtained
a rise of 3d. an hour by combination, some years
ago, rents at Miller’s Point at once went up from
Is. 6d. to 3s. per house. One sooty son of toil
remonstrated with his landlord, the owner of 40
houses, who replied : “ I’ve raised my rents
because you’ve raised your wages. You can afford
to pay more now.” “Spare my days!” replied
the “coaly,” “you blood-sucker, d’ye mean to say
I’m only workin’ for your benefit?” “ You’ve
said it,” returned Suck-’em-dry.—Yours, Labour
Member.
The evening organ of the “ wealthy lower
orders ” in Sydney affects to believe, that Premier
Dibbs does not bold republican views, as he has
taken the oath of allegiance to the Crown. To
remove the doubts of Imperial Fedcrationists The
Bulletin reproduces a sentence or two from
Dibbs’ speech on Australian Federation I
have an ambition, as a native of this country,
that this country shall become a nation. I believe
I voice the sentiments of every Australian-born
son of the soil when I say that our ambition, our
aspiration, and our hope is to see the flag of the
Southern Cross flying not only over united Aus-
tralia but over Australia united as one nation.
That is the future I aspire to as a loader in this
councry. ” On a previous occasion Dibbs impressed
upon Parkes the prudence of providing a suite of
rooms in the new Houses of Parliament for the
accommodation of the President of the Australian
Republic.
A few weeks ago sonic surprise was aroused
by the frantic somersault of a certain Foreign-
trade paper, which published fourteen bloodthirsty
articles against Edmund, say Smith, and then
issued a fifteenth, calling on the people to vote
for him as ono man and put him in. It now
transpires that the chairman of directors was the
cause of the trouble. At the last moment he sent
down his ukase, “this paper is to go strong for
Smith.” Then there was foaming and cursing in
the editorial department, and the editor could lie
heard dancing wild fandangebs of rage on the
stairs. There were three courses before them—to
resign, to write another frantic Frcctiade article
against Smith and let the chairman do his worst,
or to knock under. They decided to knock under,
and next day the paper sang a different song in
quite a different key.
The C.M.G.’s in N.S.W. Upper House, as
a rule, all roost together. John Davies and
“Bob’’Smith, good attendants, always sit side
by side, and when C. J. Roberts finds t'me to
appear ho, an a rule, sits alongside the other two.
Ned Coombes turns up occasionally and fixes him'
self immediately behind the last named. The ex-
Judges sit together, and until (heir “promotion”
Salomons and Dick O'Connor chipped in with
Pilcher, Q.C. The courtly G. B. Simpson, Q.C.,
has scarcely put in an appearance since he resigned
office. The medical men sit in bunches—Renwick,
M‘Kellar, M'Laurin and Bowker discuss influenza
and other kindred topics; Dr. Creed sits alone at
the table buried in military figures. Sheepbreeders
like Lee, Halliday and “Jack” Smith form a
coterie; the old firm of “ Pigott and Triekett ”
still exists in the Council with an occasional chip
in from “Dr.” Norton. The horsey men, Long,
Dangar and S. A. Smith are inseparable. The
only men who seem to be alone are John Lucas
and Sir Alban J. Riley.
Sample of cast-iron gall from tho alleged
Governor’s speech at the opening of Canadian
Parliament;—
I have also to express my sympathy in the regret which
you and the whole Dominion feel, and which I sincerely
share, for the lamented loss of the late Prime Minister,
Sir John Macdonald, who died full of years and honours
in the midst of your deliberations.
Think of that! The ancient father of swindles
and corruption, the prize liar of all America, the
politician whose life was one long snavel, the man
who took tips, and faked electoral-rolls, and sold
contracts, who allowed his subordinates to charge
their mistresses to the Consolidated Revenue, and
who never allowed a job for whitewashing a
Government fence to pass without getting his per-
centage out of it. Think of these things, and
consider the rhinoceros hide of the British Gover-
nor who could stand up and fire off eulogies over
the dust of “Old To-morrow!”

J. Astley Cooper (quondam London cor*
respondent to cne of the Adelaide dailies, and
now editor of Great Britain, a journal devoted
to the Imperial Federation fetich) and not his-
torian Fronde, is the real author of what the
cable-man describes as “Fronde’s scheme ” for
bringing England and her colonies into closer
communion. Mr. Fronde's name only comes into
the matter by reason of the fact that Cooper sub-
mitted the scheme to him for his approval. Mr.
Froude admits that the idea of a Federal Parlia-
ment is absurd, and the proposed commercial
union an impossibility. He says that the Ameri-
cans would join heaitily in this ideal Pan-Anglo-
Saxon Festival. Cooper has succeeded in getting
a good ad. for his paper, which, after all, is per-
haps what he wanted.
Dear Bulletin, —Henry Lawson, in his
verses “The Shame of Going Back,” in your issue
of 10th October, calls up to us Southerners the
fate of poor Joe Baxter and the equally lament-
able fates of two others —Charley Roberts and
William Thompson—who followed in his tracks.
Joe was landlord of the Pier Hotel here, and
made a comfortable little pile. Prohibition and
Local Option threatened demolition to the whisky-
trade, and Joe went farther afield. He sold out
and crossed over to plunge on the Melbourne Cup.
He plunged so deeply that he had some trouble
in getting his head above water ; then he started
a lemonade-mill in Bananaland, which brought
him below the Plimsoll-mark, and he returned to
Maoriland—to Auckland—no farther; the shame
of going back stranded him there with a bullet
through his brain. His friend, Charley Roberts,
took it badly to heart and administered to himself
a similar leaden prescription. Then William
Thompson, whilom host of the Criterion Hotel, a
friend of both the deceased, and a trustee in
Roberts’ estate, laid himself out cold with the
latter’s revolver. These are all comparatively
recent happenings, and now we don’t know where
this kind of thing will end.—Yours, &c.,
T.M.W., Dunedin, Maoriland, 10th October 1891.

SUFFERED 30 YEARS, BUT NOW WELL.
Seymour, Victoria, October 14th, 1891. For
thirty years I suffered from Liver, Kidney, and
Gravel Diseases, and it would be impossible to
give even a faint idea of the misery and pain I
endured during that time. I was treated by
several of the leading doctors in Melbourne, but
not one of them could give me any hope of a
recovery. They expected my complaints to
develop into Bright’s Disease. I was serving in
the Mounted Police force, but had to resign on
account of my illness. After taking a few bottles
of safe cure, I passed a quantity of clotted blood,
after which I had ease. I continued taking the
safe cure for twelve months and then felt I u-'dS
quite cured. My wife was afraid the disease
would return, and advised me to continue its use
for a further period in half doses, which I did.
That was about three years ago, and since then I
have not felt anything of the old complaint, and
now am able to eat, drink, sleep and work with
pleasure. I can now appreciate what health and
strength is. My friends never expected to see me
alive to-day, much less in the enjoyment of all my
bodily powers.— J. G. Doxey, Wholesale Produce
Merchant.

It will take 1,600,000 rifles to arm Russia’s
European army alone. Between the Russian and
French factories, which are hard at work day and
night, this number of weapons of the most
approved pattern will be supplied before the end
of ’92. Armageddon may come then.
The female flea never bites, unless her
young be attacked. How, then, it may be asked,
does she live? Simply by kissing her husband,
and absorbing the blood left on his moustache.
Another version is that the female flea lives by
biting her husband. Arguing from analogy (in
the matrimonial domain) this is very likely
indeed.
James Fenton, in his “Bush Life in Tas-
mania” says, regarding transportation: “Many
good farm servants — better than any that I have
seen since—used to be sent out for seven years for
mere trifles. I knew one excellent fellow who
received that sentence for stealing a turnip that
grew by the roadside, which he ate as he went
along.”
From August 15 to September 15, the
most popular work in London, as tested by book-
sellers’ sales, was Rudyard Kipling’s “Life’s
Handicap.” The agents of the Sydney and Mel-
bourne trade sent out huge supplies, which, how-
ever, don’t move off. The volume contains some
of the author’s finest work, but also a lot of
rubbish.

“ It was expected that Mr. H M. Stanley,

Item from the red-painted Booth :
They should always seek to help the sufferers, no
matter who they were. Even if in the Happy Land they
saw a fellow angel fall down and break his leg—if he had
a leg—(laughter)—they should say to another, “ hold my
crown while I help the poor fellow out of his trouble.”—
(Laughter.) Let them never mind their crowns just now,
but help those who were in need of it.
The Bulletin doesn’t want to cast aspersions
upon anybody, but it would strongly recommend
that angel with the broken-leg to feel in both bis
heavenly pockets as soon as he recovered con-
sciousness, and see if his money is all there.
Also, he should have his halo tied oh with a big
rope.
A wild-eyed damsel rushed into Melbourne
Hospital the other night, crying aloud for a deep
draught of antidote and a speedy application of
the stomach pump. It subsequently transpired
that she had been quarrelling with her young
chap on the burning subject of “ the other girl,”
and had slowly absorbed sixpen’orth of opium
with a view to being stopped before she could
accomplish her fell purpose. Unfortunately
Romeo was not equal to the occasion, or perhaps
the ardent swain had his hands in his pockets at
the time. He stood by and meditated,
apparently, whilst the injured pansy was taking
her cup of cold poison and saying “ There, now !”
over the rim, after each sip. And when all the
deadly potion had penetrated into the interior of
Miss Harriet her admirer went on smoking as
usual, so she sprinted off to the Hospital and was
pumped dry, in a dull, methodical manner. The
moral of which story is that a jealous girl
generally hates the other minx worse than
poison.

Kipling must deteriorate, if he keeps out
of his proper atmosphere, like Stevenson and
Haggard. Nathaniel Hawthorne ruined his fame
by leaving the America of the “Twice Told
Tales,” “Scarlet Letter,” and “House of the
Seven Gables.” Inexhaustible India is Kipling’s
field, and out of it he will only become a book-
maker, like Haggard out of South Africa.

Association, and a father of W. Gerard Phillips,
of Sydney, the well-known financial - agent.

of his host and hostess, and said : “Now, remem-

“Rolf Boldrewood’s” new story, “Never-
more” —part of w'hieh appeared in the defunct
Centennial Magazine— will be published in
January. Of “Robbery under Arms,” 40,000
copies have been published. The sale of his other
books to date has averaged 10,000 to 12,000 copies
each. In his publisher’s catalogue “ Boldre-
wood’s ” name is sandwiched between Aurelius
(Marcus) and Caesar (Julius). There’s fame !
When the effects of old “Bob” Waters,
the retired Bathurst officer of police, were ex-
amined by his executors, a locked tin box was
discovered in his bedroom, which, on being
opened, was found to contain two human skulls,
said to be those of notorious bushrangers. The
question is how “Bob” came by them. Were
they a part of the spoil obtained when Dan. “ dug
up Deniehy ” in the old Milltown cemetery ?
The new London literary journal, The
Bookman, issues with its first number a fine
reproduction of an early portrait of Tennyson.
Though the paper doesn’t state so, the drawing
originally appeared as the frontispiece to that
very remarkable production, The Spirit of the
Age, edited by Richard Hengist Horne, author of
“ Orion.” Horne, on leaving Melbourne for Eng-
land, gave his own copy of the publication to
Judge Pohlman, of the Victorian County Court.
A. H. Boyd, Stipendiary Magistrate of
the Huon district, Tasmania, well-known as one
of the later Port Arthur “Commandants,” was
killed the other day by a fall from his horse. His
death has removed a disgraceful state of things in
regard to the impartial administration of justice
in the district named. To fine a man for being
“drunk and incapable,” and then go home help-
lessly drunk, was but a small item in the vagaries
of this man pitchforked into position by
influential relatives.
The late Thadeus O Kane, of the
Northern Miner, (Q.), didn’t set much store on
racing matter for his paper. One November day
he told his foreman to put in a rather trivial
political paragraph and omit the result of the
Melbourne Cup, for which result a big crowd was
waiting outside the office. However, the chief
comp., who was “ a bit of a sport,” publ shed the
Cup winner and left out Thady’s political par,
and then there was trouble, as anyone who ever
knew Thadeus O’K. will readily imagine.
Bookstall Smith had a bio; funeral. Queen
Victoria sent Ponsonby and Radnor to mourn for
her. Wales sent Suffield to mourn for him. The
Duke of Edinburgh wailed through his agent —
one Colville. Louise despatched a flunkey named
Collins, and Beatrice sent another flunkey named
Biddnlph to grieve officially. Battenberg and
Connaught didn’t send anybody. Not a single
soul of any importance turned up to weep in
person. There was about half-a-mile of empty
cabs and carriages, and that was practically all.
t' A Sydney Sunday paper tells you about'
the daughter and son of Melville, the bushranger,
meeting on board the old hulk “Success” in
Sydney harbour, which would be peculiarly affect-
ing if only true. There are two persons, and two
only, who know the true secret of Melville’s
life-one an official now very high up in the
Victorian Civil Service, who has a narrative of
Melville’s career, penned by the criminal himself,
and “Price Warung,” who has a copy of that
manuscript. Melville was a clergyman’s son, and
an Oxford undergraduate. His story will be told
when his father— still alive—dies.
Some more about Dailey. The house of
Hordern, draper, in which it was proposed to
apprentice young Dailey, is still standing and
still a draper’s. The two houses built by his
father in aristocratic Macquarie-street still stand,
now old-fashioned and ricketty. When Dailey
was called to the Bar his mother, good woman,
was so proud of the fact that she frequented the
drapers’ shops asking for “lavender gloves for my
son, the barrister.” Dailey’s father and mother,
brother and sisters, are in the R.C. Cemetery in
Devonshire-street. His wife is in Billy Long’s
vault, at Randwick, and his own corpse in
Waverley.
Lord Esher, the Master of the Rolls, has
had himself and wife elegantly carved and laid
out upon the marble lid of their silent tomb in
Esher parish churchyard. There they lie in
truthful effigy, flat on their backs, and with their
calm, cold heads resting upon the same hard
bolster, to show that even in death they will not
require a pillow apiece, and fight for the one with
most feathers in it. Every Sunday morning,
Esher and My Lady walk, arm in arm, through
the churchyard to the quaint little house of
prayer, and sometimes they pause by the way to
pick slugs, or dabs of dirt, off their dead selves as
they lie a-dreaming.
The “ Autobiography of Archbishop Ulla-
thorne,” the first R.C. Vicar-General in Australia,
just published, is another illustration of the
danger of a man, even though possessed, as the
Archbishop was, of a remarkable memory and keen
powers of observation, trusting to his recollec-
tion. The book is prodigiously interesting,
viewed from almost any standpoint, but its im-
portance as a contribution to Australian history
is greatly discounted by little inaccuracies of date
and place. Many of the errors could be corrected
by references to earlier publications from the same
pen, but apparently the one book consulted by the
Archbishop was ex-Judge Therry’s “Eeminis-
cences.” Even misprints in the latter volume are
repeated in the Archbishop’s memoirs.

Society
When you go to inspect a mine in com-
pany with a man never allow him to smoke a pipe
while washing prospects. Some gold, you see,
may accidentally fall out of his pipe into the dish.
Honest men who have a habit of turning their
pipes upside-down should especially be looked
after on such occasions.
• # *
What would have been a gruesome Sydney
divorce-case has just been obviated by the death
oTE the husband. Part of the evidence forthcoming
was that the lord and master, well-known in
“Society,” came home in the d.t.’s, seized a large
carving-knife, and swore to cut the throat of the
new baby on its arrival.
• # •
Jealousy was the all-powerful cause of
nearly all the divorce-eases heard at the recent
Sydney sittings. It is also the cause of most
marriages. We collocate these facts for the benefit
of people desirous of marriage. Jealousy is the
ioker of the matrimonial euchre-pack.
• # *
At Croydon, Queensland, lately—writes a
correspondent—a young lady had two lovers and
did not know which to choose. However, she
arranged to go to Normanton. Bill was to follow
by the next train and marry her. She went, but
the arrangement reached the ears of Jack, who
immediately jumped on his horse, rode 100 miles
to Normanton without drawing rein, and heat the
train. He married the girl the same day and
took her back next day. At the station they met
the other fellow, who only then realised that he
had dropped his watermelon.

• * *
Lord Sheffield, tooled by Major WardiU,
took a sort of triumphal trot round the ground
after the Vies, had concluded their melancholy
duties at the wickets last Friday. The mob fairly
rose at the “ noble patron of cricket ” and cheered
him as though he wore Billy Elton in a new opera.
A ’orty Family Herald stamp of earl might,
under similar conditions, have been a gloomy
failure, but the cut of the Sheffieldian trousers
established his lordship as a favourite on the spot.
A lord who looks kindly and is fairly in touch
with the multitude on at least one subject is
regarded with the same amount of exaggerated
admiration that falls to the lot of a parson who
behaves like an ordinary Christian. Critically
considered, Lord Sheffield is merely a non-
aggressive representative of deplorable social and
political systems, and better deserves to be wept
and groaned over than to be cheered at. But,
regarded as a simple-minded old fellow who runs
a wholesome show for its own glory, and with an
amiable desire to amuse more people than himself,
Sheffield is not so displeasing to the democratic
eye. His title and undue wealth are faults for
which thousands of bad precedent excuses might
be quoted, and as long as he keeps his coronet in
the pocket of his baggy pants—well, we don't
mind.
• * *
The Bulletin heaves a sympathetic sigh
in advance for the doctor who may be called in to
attend a certain noble patient on a certain inter-
esting occasion of l lie near future. If he should
omit to give the high-born stranger all its titles
there’ll be the very dickens of a row. One can
fancy the abashed leech being reminded that he is
addressing “her little ladyship,” or “the honor-
able infant.”

The trooper who tried to “square” the
markers at the recent big N.S.W. Rifle meeting
has been dismissed from the service. He is lucky
in escaping without being tried for endeavouring
to obtain money by false pretences. That, any-
how, is what his offence amounted to.

A horrible story is being whispered-round
one of the N.S.W. Government departments. A
certain place-proud civil servant, whose meagre
qualifications are incompatible with his official
position, has long been sheltered under the wing
of a far higher dignitary. The favoured subor-
dinate has a pretty daughter. You know the rest.
She has been sent away , . . . but the two
gentlemen are just as friendly as ever.

who has been endeavouring to unite labour and
capital—in the shape respectively of himself and a
rather pretty girl. Part of the trouble consists in
the tact that she is still a minor whose gilded
father forbids the tltiiofl.
* * *
A clever Sydney reporter thus lost his
appointment. He was, so goes the story, sent to
report a lecture by a suburban parson, and while
the dull discourse was proceeding he rested— the
matter was too dismal even for his paper. So he
wrote out a slip and passed it on to the represen-
tative of the other daily—“ How much are_ you
making of this d d rot? I mean to cut it off
with 25 lines.” The other man held up two
fingers, meaning “ two sticks,” at which his
friend nodded approvingly, and neither took any
more notes. That evening the parson and his
friends found on the hall-floor the note quoted
above, and—and there you are! They went to
the office, and the reporter was sacked ” for
Blasphemy.

On a Sydney suburban back-door may be
seen a big inscription which reads —“ Lieut.-
Colonel M M .” Now, what we want to
see is the man who will hire a house next door
and affix thereto a glaring brass-plate with the
legend, “Partially-paid Private Jones.”
» • *
We have received Messrs T. Cook and
Sons’ “ Unicode,” a handy little book which
professes to furnish a telegraphic code to suit all
the ordinary emergencies of life. Unfortunately
the individual who drafted it seems to have had
no idea what are the ordinary emergencies of life
—his ignorance in this respect is lamentable and
awful. In all the book, for instance, there isn't a
single code word to express the sentence : “ Fly
instantly; all is discovered !” Yet that is a
message which is more in request than any other
we know of, and it wants to be sent
promptly, and in a disguU d form. Nor is
there one single form which means “ bank
burst ; no assets ; lie low, shareholders look-
ing for you with guns.” There are 179 separate
code words for racing telegrams, and not one of
them means : “ Hire Moses to stiffen the animal,”
or “Owner squared,” or “Horse poisoned as
arranged.” Then there are several dozen abbrevia-
tions for such law telegrams as “Judge has com-
menced his summing up,” or “Opinion of Counsel
is favourable,” aiid never a solitary code for
“ Engage perjurer promptly,” or “ Forge affidavit
and make liostile witness drunk,” or “ Hire
bruiser to bash plaintiff.” It isn't much of a book
when all these things are left out. At any rate,
it won’t do for Australia.
» # *
The Sydney D.T. reports this memorable
fact: —
It has recently transpired that among the employes in
the catering department of N.S.W. Parliament House is a
gentleman connected with an old and titled family of the
United Kingdom. The name of this gentleman is Mr.
, who, under an assumed name, has for
some time been employed in .the buildings. He is the
son 0 f , a cousin of the Baronet of Blanks-
town, and who is head of the house of > which is
a very ancient Irish family.
This looks so very probable that we wouldn’t be
surprised to hear that all the rest of the waiters
were baronets also. When the British nobleman
of any sort or variety goes out to earn his own
living he generally takes to rushing about with
a towel in a restaurant. The individual who is
grovelled to in England, when he finds his level in
a democratic community, straightway starts to
grovel himself, and take small tips, and wash
dishes, and become a sort of male housemaid and
potato-peeler. The billet suits him. There is an
undefinable relationship between the British aris-
tocracy and the flunkey, therefore when the
aristocrat becomes a flunkey no one notices the
difference, and when the flunkey passes himself
off as a nobleman no one notices the difference
either. Whenever a bogus earl is arrested for
cashing valueless cheques in Australia it invari-
ably transpires that he has been a valet, because
the valet looks so like an earl, and the earl looks
so horribly like a valet that they are practically
one and the same animal.
* * *
Matters are looking up with the Victorian
Artists’ Society, The committee has happily
disposed of the ghastly old sepulchre on Eastern
Hill {Melb.), and announces the opening of the
now building early in '92. The aesthetic-looking
Panton de Beerthirst, P.M., too, was last week
elected pres’dent, so, under his direction, the
inaugural ceremony is expected to give another
twist to those silken bonds. Hopetoun will be
received with a full band.
# * *
Talking of Victorian art, a few local men
have turned out a cycloramic picture, “The
Defeat of the Spanish Armada,” which is certain
to make a sensation at the Tasmanian Exhibition,
Even when considered on “the straight,” this
long marine subject is a genuine success. When
completed, toned down with gunpowder smoke
and duly fixed-up behind a bold, rocky fore-
ground, it should prove a first-class shilling show.
As a sea piece “The Armada” is a novelty in
oycloramas, also, it has the advantage of calling
attention to the revered idea that Britain
rules the waves. To this end the designer has
been careful to give the combatants a smooth sea
and no favour, instead of throwing in all the luck
on the weaker side. The immense superiority of
one Englishman over forty Spaniards can
be taken in at a glance. Mr. G. F. Yenner-
mark, who has painted an attractive ocean and
sky, designed the scheme of battle ; Fred. Knee-
bone, Rccknell, Gordon Coutts, and Hugh
Patterson, being responsible for most of the fore-
ground, galleons, figures, and etceteras. The
Vagabond is understood to have written the usual
threepenny pamphlet, whilst an Elizabethan
lecture on the Armada and its destruction has
been constructed, on scientific principles, by
Victor Daley.

torian Women’s Christian Temperance Union
That we be careful in all our meetings to discounten-
ance everything of the nature of a theatrical performance,

ciples and dangerous to our young people.
Surely the race of non-theatre-goers ought to be
nearly extinct by this time !

A PROFESSIONAL GENTLEMAN, a Widower,
wishes to meet an intelligent LADY, who would
take an active interest in his business. E.K.A.,
G.P.0., Sydney.
Why advertise for such a woman? She is every-
where. Every woman considers herself intelli-
gent, and every woman takes an active interest in
every man’s business.
w * •
The la ( est Victorian Artists’ Exhibition
is chiefly remarkable for a humorous catalogue,
in which the exhibitors have set prices upon their
gems of art. Gordon Frazer, for instance, asks a
mere trifle of .£(500 for his “ Cannibal Feast in
the Island of Tauna,” an “ unfinished ” study of a
sort of walk round by a number of washed-oub
niggers, in the depths of a new tint of green
forest. A colossal apparition, which may be
intended for a live heathen, or a wooden god,
stands erect in the left foreground, and the diffi-
culty of discovering what part this bloated mon-
strosity plays in the corroboree constitutes the
charm of the picture, and has, presumably, a lot to
do with its price. Explorer Stanley was brought
up in triumph by Gordon Frazer to view this
great work, but he tore himself away without
producing a cheque-book and buying the treasure
on the spot. Another waggish item on the cata-
logue is £l7O for M'Cubbiu’s pure Aus-
tralian subject, “The Runaway.” There aie
three human figures and an alleged horse
visible on the fair-face of M'C.’s landscape,
but the runaway (presumably a bullock, a goat, or
a wild, untamed rooster) seems lost in the distance.
Interest centres in a clean-shaven actor, or gentle-
man fresh from gaol, who has taken to the
bush because one of his arms is two yards long
from shoulder to elbow, and in the back view of
an aged person with a hand like a loin chop cut to
a point at the scrag end. Tennyson Cole's water
colour “ In Marble Halls ” is another cheap thing.
The subject is seemingly a bright-faced parlour-
maid draped in a yellow bed-curtain. She is a
nice girl enough in her way, and might be trusted
to clean up and do mo tof the washing for twelve
shillings a week, though in the capacity of a high-
toned Toorakian at £2(52 10s. she strikes one as a
failure. If the price of a picture is what it will
fetch there can be no doubt that none of the
exhibits in the V.A.E. are priceless. Some of
them are well worth taking at catalogue figures,
and others will always fetch a trifle as curiosities.

Thargomindah (Q.) a-way-out-West town,
is to be lighted by electricity. Why do the cities
lie behind ? Because the syndicate goeth up and
down seeking whom he may devour, and the City
Councilman and the newspaper proprietor are his
jackal.
# * *
A Sydney Sunday paper publishes an item
this week on Feet. It begins as follows
While a nightly bath is excellent, the feet should not
be soaked oftener than once a week.
We fell asleep before we got any further, and
carried that item with us into our dreams. And
then we fancied that we were wandering by the
solemn-sounding ocean, and in a secluded bay,
where the waters rippled gently on a beach of
yellow sand, we came across a Sunday paper
washing its feet. The rest of the dream is unfit
for publication.

Yet further additions to the N.S.W. Bar.
There is room at the top for good men ; there is
room at the top of King-street for failures. And
18 attorneys were last week licensed to practise at
one fell swoop. Most of them can only get a
living as money-lenders.

There have been some very lively court
altercations of late between members of the Tas-
manian bar, especially during the hearing of the
case Edwards v. the Queen. The personal dispute
which occurred between Mr. Byron Miller and
Mr. C. S. Cansdell, late of Sydney (who got into
the Tasmanian Legislative Assembly about 20
years ago on the Main Line railway advocacy
ticket and who then elected to leave Tasmania,
where he did not turn up again till a year or two
ago) was extremely edifying.

The alleged hard case of a young Mel-
bourne constable, who married a pub. with land-
lady attached, has just come to light. Under
police regulations, no member of the force can
hold an interest in a licensed house, consequently
this unfortunate victim of circumstances must
either retire from a life of ease and devote himself
to drawing beer, or deny on affidavit that he
takes any sort of interest in his bride. Mean-
while, the lady feels aggrieved on the ground that
she married a policeman, and naturally considered
that his uniform was to become a part of herself.
# # #
A report that the first Victorian-bred
majah of the local artillery intends to throw up
his billet sounds too good to be true. A man who
is loaded up with military spirit seldom fires him-
self off in that sensible way.

Adelaide newspapers for hinting that Lord Shef-
field, “ that noble patron of the game,” can’t make
a good speech. They ought not to make game of
the noble patron.

“Songs of the South."
Of the multitude of books there is no end ; and
most books had been better never written,
never printed and never read. Nearly every-
thing that is worth doing has been done ’better
than anyone can expect to cjfl it>, but this con-
sideration Inis 1191 pr'eVeflied Mr. John Bernard
HHarA’, M.A., from putting forth a little
volume which he calls “ Songs of the South ”
presumably touching Australia somewhat,
though a very careful examination fails
altogether to show in what particular—occult
or otherwise. “ Songs of the South ” arc really
not songs at all, or “ poems ” in any sense what-
soever. They are, indeed, very decent samples
of vacation verse-=tho sort of verse One Wight
expect from a young man With a fairly correct
ear and a turn for rhyming, accustomed to cilll
Ids gods and goddesses from the pages flf
Bempriere’s “ Glassical Dictionary;’ and to Veil
the ordinary Operations of Nature ih the
SOrtorous epithets cribbed from the cheap
elaaaicisirt of a memorised mythology.
* # ' #
A perusal of Mr. O’Hara’s pages reveals
quite a wealth of classical syllables. Here are
a few : Amphion, Aurora, Phosphor, Orion,
Atlas, Apollo, Oceanus—he is strong on Apollo
and Oceanus Hesper, Hesperian, lonian,
Amalthea, Hades, Mysian, iHflllisj ifMati;
Kuroclydon. Hypetbpreun, Chloe, Lipara,
Biiropa-, Hesperidfls, Neptune, Dian, &c., &c.,
He speaks rapturously of ‘ ‘ lonian waters,”
the Naiads, the Sphinx, “ Syrian years,”
“ Hades’ portal,” Lotus-ease, the Nymphs,
“ Elysian bowers,” et hoc genus omne ; and his
clouds are not clouds, but “swift Nimbi.”
When he desires a variation of imagery he
finds it in such phrases as “ the Galiph of the
East ” and “ Pharaoh’s kine-.” He is strflngely
dumb on the purely- Australian side, bht waxes
weirdly eloquent aiient Hy-Sruzil uud El-
dorado, and finds a remarkable pleasure in
apostrophizing Azrael and the Yule-King. In
short, Mr. O’Hara iS anything but Australian.
His verse is the poetry Of lordly and florid
adjectives and imported stock phrases re-
miniscential of much reading df the English
poets of Gray 9 time, when no man could write
half-a-dozen lines without as many invocations
to the Muses, or the tuneful Nine, or the rosy-
fingered Dawn.
* * »
Mr. O’Hara revels in “ hyaline waters,” and
“ eastern gales ” that “ brook ” the presence of
ships. He remembers “halcyon days;” he
sings “ the holy hymn of hills s” he wanders in
“dreamy dells ” and “ bdsky dells >” and when
he lies dflwfl at flight he iildulges in “ ambrosial
SlhUp. ’ ThroUghodt his book we hear of little
gls'e than “ tasselled wheat” and “babbling
brooks “ shining zones ” and “shining oriels;”
“ singing seas ” and “ astral flowers ;” “ glanc-
ing prows ” and “ honeyed hours.” He grows
aesthetic over “amorous springs,” “ Adonian
bloom,” and “ Zidonian days.” He tells of
“what time the empyrean glowed,” and how
Aurora, “ the goddess, trode bright rosy to her
golden car.” Ho calls the sun “ the oriflamme
of day,” and he speaks also of “ the sun-god’s
gleaming eir so Aurora lias not a monopoly
of vehicular transit. In Mr. O’Hara’s pages,
“ the starlets flee and dip their foreheads iri the
wave,” “orient pyres ’ Hood all the Eastern
§Uy, which glows like “lustrous haunts of
Paradise.” Up and down his verses “the
autumnal rose-down blows,” and “fringing
foam flowers ” festoon them everywhere.
* # #
Mr. O’Hara’s volume is the true college-bdy’s
vacation-poetry exercise, and there: is very little
of “the Soflth” in it, save hete and there a
reference to “ trackless Austral Wilds, ” In a
poem called “The Wreck,” twenty years ai e
alluded to as “twenty suns”—for, from the
context, he certainly medns years. The bulk of
the book is devoted to the month of October—
but why ? Are there not in Australia half-a-
dozen months with very similar characteristics ?
There is very little that is quotable, because
there is hardly anything Australian in Mr.
O'Hara’s verse. He does certainly allude
rather weakly to the “ wattle’’—but this is
hardly a recommendation to our wattle-wearied
cars. His poem, “In Memory of Henry Ken-
dall,” begins thus gruesomely :
Once more revolves the sounding key,
The bolts of death are drawn—
awkwardly suggesting to people who know no
better that the bard was gibbeted out of life
in the good old fashion. This poem, says the
Loudon Saturday Review, is worthy of Ken-
dall himself. Which is very sad, and only shows
what they think of our literature “at Home,”
In “A Christmas Tryst ” the poet rather
ineffectively deals—(in the three last stanzas) —
with the incident so daintily treated by John
Farrell in his “ Bell of the Ly-ee-moon.” Long-
fellow found that one cannot make poetry out of
unmeaning native phrases, and “Hiawatha” is
nothing but a splendid failure. Mr. O’Hara
essays an “Aboriginal Death-song,” in which
he repeats Longfellow’s mistake, and thereby
spoils something that promises better in the
opening lines. Of course, there is the usual
versified drivel over the fate of Gordon, who
seems to awaken nearly as much interest in the
poet’s breast as Oceanus or Phosphor—whom,
we are very sorry to learn—are now quite dead.
Certain English critical journals, to wit : the
Saturday Review, the World, the Spectator,
the Speaker, the National Observer, and the
Review of Reviews, express opinions widely
different from those held by The Bulletin
concerning Mr. O’Hara’s verse, but this is
doubtless due to several not very occult causes,
and only show how very “advanced” the
publisher’s trade is becoming. The volume is
well-printed and tastefully behind; and is otheF
wise deserving of a better fate.

a week or two ago, Miss Mary Canty, whp ntns

A little. Sdhg all td Jletseiqin a private loom; and

DRY.
UNNECESAR “They say he died
drunk. Just his bloomin’ luck, ’spose I shall die as sober as a donkey.”
put his immense ears in at the door, and took an

The only consolation which the injured lady has

There are various ways of doing it, but probably
the shortest and most efficacious would be to hire
a lovely slavey and let her—for a consideration —
win the officer's heart, and then jump on his
blighted affections with both feet.

the disquiet of his fellow prisoners and the exas-
peration of thb warders; who endeavoilt jh vaili to
detect and “surround” him. He can throw his
voice into any cell. Whilst the chief warder in

Dear Bulletin, —Why should not the man
who drinks liquor be licensed as well as the man
Whd sells it? The ‘‘liquor trade” mhst tldcds-
jiaWlsr consist of and d.rihkei' as and
fact, the drinker is trie qiore of trie twdj
and when the State goes in for “ regulating ” the
liquor-traffic, it is a woeful absurdity to regulate
only one-half of it. There would be a large
revenue to be obtained by compelling every
drinker to take out a permit, and pay a fee—the
fee to vary according to the kind of liquor he
takes. For instance, a man might acquire the
right to drink “colonial ’’beer and Australian wine
at a rnoderate figure, and then the rates eould go
steadily up|kdro, till at wife top of the list balhe
the man who had the right to drink everything;
Every drinker would have to show his badge
before being supplied, and the man drinking
without a ticket would come under the same
category as the man travelling by rail without his
teboard. Any publican selling drink to a
reason who had no ticket would be fined. The
basis of this idea is that when it takes two men to
break a law they ought both to stand on the same
footing. If the courts fine the man who runs a
consultation, they should also fine every man who
takes a ticket in it; when they gaol the three-card
individual they should also gaol the man who
plays with him ; and when they jump on the pub-
lican who sells after hours they should likewise
jump on the citizen who drinks after hours. The
consultation individual can’t run his business
unless somebody takes tickets ; and the three-
card fiend can’t play his game unless fomebody
plays with him ; and the publican can’t sell after
hours unless somebody drinks after hours. Neither
can any man sell liquor without a license unless
somebody buys liquor from a man without a
license ; and if it is fair and reasonable to penalise
the person who does one-half of the transaction,
it is equally reasonable for the man who does the
other half.— Titus.

The Theatre Royal Hotel, Sydney, has
lately changed hands, and is now under entirely
new management. A splendid stock of wines,
spirits, liqueurs and cigars has been laid in, and
an altogether new feature is-provided in the shape
of an American bar, where all the strange, curious
and refreshing drinks known in the United States
can be obtained. Their name is legion, but among
them may be mentioned champagne cocktails,
gin fizz, milk punch, mint julep—probably the
noblest liquor known to man—imperial punch,
Manhattan cocktails, and many others. All
mixed by American bar-keeps. James A. Walsh,
proprietor, late of the Occidental Hotel, San
Francjsco r #

“A Race that Can Stand the Climate."
Dear Bulletin, - Is it presumptuous for me to differ from
Rudyard Kipling on the subject of India ? Things must
have altered in 29 years if you can get a. race by jhtfet®
marriage with the natives;, Let hie fjjtplain ivhat I Ifieah;
The Inspector-General of Hospitals in the very loku’s of
Mr. Kipling told me that the Eurasian, or half-caste rate;
did not, as a rule, survive the third generation, except irl
a puny, imbecile or vicious pattern ; that they threw back
to the more impotent type of their progenitors and vir-
tually failed. Now, this may, by hygiene, manners,
moials, and education, have been altered; otherwise it
would be necessary to find, from the best specimens
of the two races, males for marriage with the females
of the opposite colour, and a constant supply
of the Saint, or ttt establish haras, but, of course, both
plans tvbuld be illegal and in violation of current ideas.
It is known well in India that country-bred horses are
valueless as sires at the stud. Even those bred in the
studs of Haupper, Kurnaul and Buxar are relegated to
the list. Again, the Eurasian caialry or Lahore Light
Horse Were a lamentable failure, I believe, for immorality.
Here let hie state that the Commanding officer cut his
throat bn the dissolution bf the eorpS; I got his im-
ported spaniel EloSs; ,knd a lot of his fyihb; Aiid fvhjteS
object much to marry half-castes. A lady i knew died
because her children were chee-chees; she never knew
that her fair-haired husband was a Eurasian. And again,
the natives want either a white or one of their own caste.
Certainly the Rajah of P married a Eurasian. Result,
separation. I could suggest a far better plan (but it is
too late) than R. K.’s. If the old shakers of the Pagoda-
breed had been left under tender John Company: if
racial religion and distinction had been encouraged, and
the ancient customs of the Hindoos, Mussulmans, et hoe
Wins mm, been left as fount), then England (curse it 1
Ss with. Stanley, It’s Englahd; nbt Btitaih); then I say,
England could have held the power. You know what
John Price said about convicts—“ One would watch the
other.” I think flock-masters will understand what I
have been driving at when I ask them this. Do they ever
use cross-bred rams or ewes as parents for the type of
sheep that they want to breed? Mind you, I only ask for
information. I am not a sheepy man. I never notice
stud cross-brcds offered for sale. So let Kipling’s England
(I’m a Scotchman) trust to her soldiers of'all sorts. Now
that there is much education among the natives, I fancy
that they have savet enough to prefer John Bull
(including Pitt and Sandy) to the Bear, but 1 hope and (if
I pray) I pray that when the battle of Dera Ishraail
Khan, or Serinugger, or Bhimbur, takes place between
England (as before) and Russia, dear Bulletin, I say I
pray that your special correspondent will be—Yours.
Hiram Abifp, late of the Punjab.

“ Braeside ” Allen need not alarm himself
about the rabbits walking over the top of the
“stiffnrs” into Queensland. When the rabbit-
proof fence was being erected between Barringun
and Hungerford, rabbits were regularly caught
and deposited on the Bananaland side to save
them from becoming extinct in that sinful country
Since the vines supplied to the Austrian
Government by America have turned bht to be
infected by phyloxera, Australian vignerons will
hardly know where to obtain vines free from the
insect. Perhaps few of our wine-producers are
aware that, in the vicinity of the Hodgkinson,
North Queensland, a wild vine flourishes. It is
similar in habit to the cultivated variety, growing
bunches of edible grapes devoid of acidity, like all
tropical fruits.
A new-chum lately received a letter from
England, and, being unable to read, took it to the
local publican. The publican w y as no better
scholar than the bumpkin, but, disguising his
ignorance, he opened the letter and broke into a
loud guffaw. “Great fun at home, Bill,” he
exclaimed; “can’t read it for laughing. Get
Jack, the blacksmith, to go through it.’’ The
letter apprised the recipient of his mother’s death,
so, after Jack had gone through its pages, Hodge
returned and wiped the verandah with the grinning
publican.
Avoid the “well dressed” person, given
to wearing a heavy frock-coat on a stifling mid-
summer day, wdio systematically refers to V.R.
as “ Her Majesty.” That man is either a dreary
ass, or a professional humbug. Avoid him as you
avoid his moist, unpleasant first cousin, tbe man
who calls a bad sermon “an excellent discourse.”
Also avoid the humbler, but “pushing” indivi-
duals, who won’t think of keeping their seats in a
railway-carriage whilst he of the heavy frock-coat
is standing. And, above all, steer clear of the ill-
conditioned fraud of a radical, who boasts of his
ignorance, makes an everlasting parade of bis
grimy hands, and talks of a Republic as a place
where all men spit alike.
Dear Bulletin, — Apropos your paragraph
a few weeks back re Maytown, N.Q., and its
Chinese storekeepers, why Maytown only ? The
same applies to most or nearly all northern town-
ships where I have been. At L——, a mining
township, where I visited recently, and where
unionism was much en evidence, many members,
including the local union secretary, dealt with
Chinese storekeepers, Europeans being equally
handy. The baker was a Chow, with a nigger for
mate. One morning I had occasion to call early
at the “bakery”—a wretched hovel, ten feet
square. On a rough and rather “multy ’’-looking
table was the bread, and immediately over, about
three feet above, on a bunk composed of a few
loose slabs, were the Chow and his colored mate en-
joying an early morning smoke, the roof being
evidently used as a spittoon ! And this baker was
patronised by unionists ! Yet the “Chinesemust
go”—of course they must. —Yours, Argonaut.
