NEW BONDI AQUARIUM.
Mr. Alfred Wyburd.
Mr. Alfred Wyburd, Proprietor of the New
Bondi Aquarium, the sole cause of that estab-
lishment’s greatness, is a handsome man with
a happy smile, and full of velocity, whose
feet are for ever racing after his head and never
overtaking it. Anybody who wants Wyburd can
find him by sitting down on a doorstep, in any
part of the globe, at any hour of the day or night,
and within fifteen minutes that gentleman is sure
THE NEW THEATRE.
to go past. He never goes to bed, and seldom has
time to eat. In his younger days - nobody knows
how old he is, but judging by all he has done and
all the places he has visited he should be about
200—he was one of the champion rinkists of the
earth, and many other things besides. He
appeared as a skater at Montreal jn 71; he was
a journalist in Maoriland,' and' cast a radi-
„„„„ ,1 „
ance over the suburban borough council
that met on nights of mud and slush to
consider how the members could best asphalt
their back yards out of the rates, and also
CLUB BILLIARD ROOM.
gave skating exhibitions throughout the province;
he became rinking champion of Australia in
1878, when he managed the Sir Joseph Banks,
r. , Tv.Tc.tnT , J
tany, N.S.W., for Mr. Frank Smith ; and
afterwards made a sensational appearance in
London. Then he rinked all over Europe and the
more civilised parts of Africa, and, reappearing in
Sydney, he opened the Protestant Hall as a skat-
ing establishment, and after that he roamed all
through this continent and most of Asia, and then
drifted back once more to Australia. During
1889 ho was manager of the Recreation Grounds
THE NEW BUILDING.
at Ashfield, of the Crystal Palace Skating
Rink, York-street, and of the Coogee Palace
Aquarium, Sydney, and is now, as already
mentioned, the presiding genius of the vast Bondi
Aquarium—the biggest and most varied show in
N. S. Wales. These are the smaller and balder
items of Mr. Wyburd’s history, but in addition
he has done nearly everything else that one man
could do, and he has been everywhere, and
inserted his shiny hat and inventive brain into
nearly every mystery between earth and heaven.
To re-build and re-fit the Aquarium has cost
something like =£7ooo. This sum, of course, in-
|^^“ pelldlture on improvements to ground
’ # # #
The main bllildinß itse lf i 3 muc h larger
than the structure of former days, affording
25ft greater floor space in the main hall, while
the stage has been increased in size to that of the
Criterion Theatre, and is now 10ft. deeper, sft.
higher) and Bft> wider than before the fire _ The
ma in hall is floored with 4in. kauri planking, and
every attention has been paid to making it a first-
class floor for those who delight in dancing. There
is also a dress circle which permits of the accom-
modation of a large number of visitors who
ma y nofc desire to tal “ P art j' n the Proceed-
mgs except as spectators. This circle is of
the greatest convenience as it is totally
distincfc from the body of the halL Xhe
bold glass fronts of the fish tanks give way
to fantastic rocky arches, preserving in feature
the natural waterworn characteristics of the
stone | is f ed - and this idea will also be observed in
several fountains which at intervals appear to
relieve the eye . The large f ountain) which will
be remembered by frequenters of Bondi, has been
replaced by a refreshment-room of handsome pro-
portions and designs, and any visitor and his
family will at once notice that he and his belong-
ings can now enter all at once in consequence of
the increased size of the entrances, instead of
having to creep in by detachments as in the days
before the fire. The larger entrances have also
the effect of making the various halls and divisions
brighter. The aquarium is sure to prove more
practical than heretofore. The latest improve-
ments have been introduced in the way of
aerating the tanks, and the tanks themselves will
display—if one may suppose that a fish has the
study of comfort at heart—a study for the comfort
of the fish which are to be incarcerated in them.
VIEW FROM THE GLEN.
Surmounting the whole building is a new dome
45ft. in height, which greatly adds to its appear-
ance. The grounds have been still further im-
proved for the accommodation of picnic parties
and general amusement. The skating rink has
been placed in thorough order, and although
skating is not the “ rage ” it was a few j r ears ago,
no holiday goes by at Bondi but the rink has its
full share of votaries.
* * *
Two viewsinthesketeh are representations of the
billiard-room and social-hall attached to the club-
house. Around this building runs an exception-
ally wide verandah from which some grand views
CLUB SOCIAL HALL.
are obtainable, both sea and landscape.
* # *
Therecentdisastrousfireoccurredon July 11 last.
On July 14 the contract was signed with Messrs.
Bignell and Clark for erection of new building,
and on the same day work was commenced to erect
the present palace. Too much credit cannot be
given to the Commercial Union Assurance Cos.
(Mr. J. St. Vincent Welch, manager) who rein-
stated the building, thus taking all trouble off the
shoulders of the proprietor in the re-erection, and
for the quickness and despatch with which all
claims were settled. It may be mentioned that
no sooner had the Commercial Union signed the
contract for the rebuilding, than they cabled to
England to have specially manufactured the
peculiar heavy plate-glass of lin. in thickness,
which has now been placed in position in the
tanks within seven days of arrival of same by
mail steamer. The public may be surprised to
learn that the cost of these pieces of plate-glass
and their fittings was no less than .£6OO. Mr.
Alfred Wyburd, the enterprising proprietor of
these most beautiful pleasure-grounds, has left no
stone unturned to make the Aquarium and the
rest of his property a haven for the weary, and
the very spot for seeking pleasure, health and
recreation.
To-day (Thursday) the new Aquarium will
be officially opened under the patronage* of the
Mayor and Aldermen of Sydney, the United
Grand Lodge of Freemasons, Lord Sheffield, and
the English team of cricketers, including the
famous veteran, Dr. W. G. Grace, the N.S.W.
Cricketing Association, and the cycling, football,
rowing and yachting clubs of Sydney. Also, by
way of lending further eclat to the occasion, there
will be present
Geo. Rignold and Company of Her Majesty’s
Theatre.
Messrs. MacMahon and the Evangeline Company
of the Theatre Royal.
Messrs. Brough and Boucicault and Company of
the Criterion Theatre.
Messrs. Hiscocks and Wilson and Miss Jenny
Lee’s Company, of the Garrick Theatre.
Mr. Frank Smith and his full company from the
Alhambra and the Sir Joseph Banks, Botany,
Superintendent Bear and the Metropolitan Fire
Brigades,
and nearly everybody else of any public import-
ance. The orchestra of the Theatre Royal will
render the overture of “Evangeline,” conducted
by Mr. Rice, the composer, and no effort will be
spared to make the shew the greatest of its kind
upon record.

Old Cockburn, the Sydney auctioneer of a
quarter of a century ago, had occasion once to
appear before Judge Cheeke in the Supreme Court.
Old “Cocky” was known to have been “sent
out,” and the opposing counsel—Mathew Henry
Stephen—was anxious to have the fact established.
As Cockburn never denied it, that was not hard
to establish, but M.H.S. was anxious to know
also who he was. Cockburn drew himself up to
his full height and said; “My name is Henry
Digby Cockburn, son of Alexander Cockburn,
grandson of Sir Alexander Cockburn and of Lord
Lyttelton, and brotlu r of the Right Honorable Sir
Alexander Cockburn, Lord Chief Justice of Eng-
land —better blood than flows in your old father’s
veins.” “ Tut, tut 1” growled old Chctke ; “you
must not talk like that.” But he enjoyed the
“ scraping ” also, though Mathew Henry did not.
Old Cockburn, whatever his Old-Country fault
might have been-it was a paltry one enough—
was highly respected in commercin' Sydney. He

Let it be distinctly understood that this is no
scheme of quackery to draw money out of the
afflicted, nor is it any sort of a patent medicine
humbug, since there is not a grain of medicine of any
kind required in the treatment. Notwithstanding
this fact, it takes directly hold of the worst cases
of constipation, dyspepsia, liver complaints, head-
aches, heart disease, incipient consumption,
diabetes, and Bright’s disease of the kidneys, in-
cluding fevers and inflammation of the lungs, and
of other internal organs—not by attacking these
so-called diseases directly, but by radically neutra-
lising and removing their causes, thus allowing
nature herself to do her work unimpeded.
We now simply repeat that the new treatment
acts so radically and fundamentally upon the vital
circulation without the least danger to the most
delicate, constitution, that there is not a form of
disease that flesh is heir to, which will not be
'.affected favorably to the patient by an intelligent
use of this method of treatment.
In selling this Pamphlet we are obliged to re-
a “Pledge of Honor” from those purchas-
ing it, not to show or reveal its contents outside
of their own families. This is for our own protec-
tion, and as the only means by which our just
rights in the discovery can be maintained.
Doctors, of course, who purchase are not debarred
from using the treatment in their own practice,
providing they do not show the pamphlet or do not
reveal the rationale of the treatment.

Dr.’ Stevens, of Syracuse, N.Y., was the first
M.D. to whom we revealed the new treatment-
more than 20 years ago. In writing to a friend,
about a year ago, he gives the following
* * * “Nearlv twenty years ago in this city Dr. A.
Wilford Hall gave me privately a detailed history of a
hygienic treatment he had discovered and adopted some
twenty years before, and which he had practised con-
tinuously upon himself up to that time. The history
included an account of the failure of the health, the
reasonings and conclusions which led him to adopt the
treatment, his rapid restoration to health, &c. From the
time of his first mention of it to me I have been entirely
satisfied that it saved him from a lingering consumption
and death ; and that in the years following, as I have evi-
dence to satisfy me, its continued practice has so affected
the nutrient and eliminating functions as to give to his
physical structure a greater degree of solidity, or, in
other words, a higher specific gravity than that possessed
by any other man living. I learn that he has persistently
continued the treatment to the present time - forty years
in all-and that his present health and vigorous condition
may rightfully be compared to the ‘ bloom of youth,’
though he is nearly seventy years of age. The tests I
have made of the treatment upon myself and in my prac-
tice in the twenty years past, have convinced me that
great practical benefit may be derived by members of the
medical profession and others from a careful study of the
rationale of the treatment as discovered by Dr. Hall, the
details of which he proposes to give in his forthcoming
book. A debt of gratitude will surely be due to him for
whatever scientific advances he may be able to unfold in
the line of assisting nature to ward off disease,and in the
restoration of health.— R. F. Stevens, M.D.”

Two Sides of the Galah Mountain.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Chap. I,— The Sunny Side.
Ihe sun rises redder over the Barrelbung Plain,
glares more fiercely on the northern side of the
Calah Mountain, and sets apparently at Geebung
Swamp with a more virulent final flash than it
would dare to do if justice and equality were its
mottoes.
So thinks the look-out man who, from October
till March, is sent by the manager of Dummigat
station to look out for bush-fires. The highest
point of the mountain is a trigonometrical station,
and there a whirly, mia-mia, bush-house, or what-
ever you like to call it, has been erected by suc-
cessive boundary-riders, who have given a week’s
notice because “the job is hell.” Such has been
the time-honoured expression that has deterred
many a good man from applying for a billet at
Uummigat. Not so, however, with Brandy
Malone, who was sent by chance as an emergency-
man when the tenth sentry had cursed the Galah
and died, as far as employment there for the
future was concerned.
Brandy Malone was not so called from love of
the spirit named, but only to distinguish him from
W hisky Malone, whose nose and name betray his
failing. This same Malone said he liked the job,
was willing to finish the season at it instead of in
the paddocks, and was public!y, in an audience of
ai^ ce<^ . ky Gentle Machree, the manager.
Mr. Machree is a terror to swear.
So Brandy Malone took an axe and improved
the shelter-house ; and a book, which he did not
read; and a billy and pannikin, and a water-bag,
and rode slowly every morning up to the Look-out,
where he remained till sundown.
He had been a month at this game, when about
three in the afternoon he saw a pillar of smoke
just over Geebung Swamp. At 3.15 he arrived at
a gallop to warn the manager and men, but no
manager or men were there. The cook even was
away--all gone to the fire except a crippled pen-
sioner who got a pound a week for not living on a
selection which he had taken up.
This man told Brandy Malone that the Geebung
people had sent over about 11 a.m. for all hands
and the cook, as well as a water-cart, and any
available grog, because the cultivation paddock
was in a blaze.
Ihen Malone pondered, and finally made up his
mind to wait at Uummigat.
I think I said that Mr. Machree was called the
Gentle One in irony. He was not gentle, and
hardly genteel that night. lie accused Malone of
sleeping, of being away at a pub., or reading, and
told him to go to etcetera out of this. Here endeth
Malone s career as servant to Mr., alias the Gentle,
Machree.
Chapter ll.— The Shady Side.
No more fertile or blessed spot exists in this
province than the southern side of the Galah. The
hill is so-called because all along the creek
at its base, millions of galah parrots chatter and
talk and build nests and breed and rear more
chatterers. The land is good, and the people
moderately honest. Farmers have taken up
ground, and their sons and daughters; yes, nieces
and nephews ; even blind cousins and widowed
poor relations, but none of them have such a
scope of country as the Hosts, an apt name,
because they are countless. The last 040 acres
tacked to the clan is at the bottom of the Galah.
A niece, just out from the old land, had paid her
(.) deposit for this piece of land, and slept on it
once or twice a year, not oftener, because it was so
lonely. She was gradually becoming acclimatised,
and could ride, milk, cut up a sheep, and bake
bread, when an extraordinary change took place
in her ways. She became religious ! Then she
said to her Aunt: “Aunty Kate, dear Aunty,
don’t think me crusty, or cronk, or cranky.
Conscientiously, I must and will live on
my selection. I cannot say that I live
there unless I really do.” This was about a month
before the big fire at Geebung Swamp. Aunt
Host rather admired the girl for this ; Uncle Host
called her a d d idiot; Cousin Jack agreed
with his father; Cousin Mary agreed with the
mother; but Bella Host went to live on her
square-mile at the foot of the Galah.
Chap. lll.— The Summit.
“Then we’ll go to-morrow to the township and
get married by the Police Magistrate. If you
like we’ll get a parson afterwards.” “ All right,
Brandy, dear ; and won’t uncle and the rest dance
a bit ! Good gracious, look at the smoke over
the Geebungs!”
LATEST EDITION:
FAREWELL.
Gentle Machree was about to mount his horse,
when Brandy Malone rode up. It was just after
breakfast, but then the Gentle One always drank
Bass’s ale for breakfast. Nothing makes a man
swear at 7.30 a.m. so much as breakfasting at
seven on porridge and beer. Mr. Ma’one looked
clean and neat and nice. He wore a blue necktie
and leggings. Of course he had other togs on,
but the tie and the spats were the mo_t catching.
To him Mr. Machree: “What the-what the—
what—d you, what do you want?”
“ I just rode over to say that there’s about 20
of your sheep among mine, and you might send a
man to fetch them. They're hand-drafting them
now.” This was not true ; she was doing the hand-
drafting, not they.
“ What blanky sheep ? Where have you sheep ?
Signs on it ! Send I may live ! What do you
mean ? ”
Bub Brandy Malone, being in a necktie and leg-
gings, acted the gentleman—giving the soft
answer that turneth away wrath, thus ; “ At my
little station, called Undercliff, just on the other
side of the Galah ! 11l keep them in.till midday.”
That afternoon Mr. Machree had a fit.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
W. G. Grace is “starred,” in three-feet capitals
on the Melbourne hoardings. Across the veteran’s
gigantic name is pasted a long, slim announcement that
Lord Sheffield’s team will appear on certain dates. This
is no doubt the most business-like way of advertising the
new show, seeing that R. S. Smythe is content to let the
postered name of “Stanley ’’speak for itself. Grace is a
Digger man than Stanley amongst any English-speaking
community, and a far more interesting performer to boot.
» * *
Melbourne city fathers tried their hardest to
make an ass of W. G. Grace at the Town-hall reception.
Not content with luring him into the municipal throne-
chair and hanging chains round his broad shoulders, they
wanted to perpetrate the elephantine foolery of a
“ swearing-in,” for which the champion was not ‘ on,
however. He looked, in fact, as though he thought he
hadn’t been dragged there for the purpose of inciting a
mob of stoutish gentlemen-very few of whom could hit
a haystack with a cricket-ball, or a ball with a haystack
to indulge in elevated civic “humour” at his expense.
These “ cordial receptions” become very trying when the
weather is warm and the cordial is unlimited and gratis.
* # #
The hopes of the N.S.W. team in the approach-
ing matches with Grace & Cos. are based on a sticky
wicket, and Chas. Turner, Esq., escaping the influenza.
Ferris (now turned Englishman) Turner’s fellow agent in
Sydney victories, has left no successor. The only other
tried bowler is Charlton, who at present is in better
batting than bowling form. The N.S.W. batsmen have
to face Lohmann, Attewell, Peel, Briggs, Grace, and
Sharpe—all veteran trundlers, so the match seems like
heigho! ho ! for the Britishers. The N.S.W. team have
evinced little enthusiasm. One fine afternoon lately only
Jones and Charlton, out of the 20 selected to practice,
turned up at the “ Asso.”
• • *
After the generation of Murdoch, Spoff., the
Bannermen, Palmer, M'Donnell, and so on, it is as with
extinct volcanoes.
* * •
It is suggested that, as a means of reviving
interest in cricket, an interprovincial “old identities”
match be played in Sydney on an early date.
• # •
Dear Bulletin, — Dave Soott, the Melbourne
cricket “ Almanac,” has the parochial “ cheek ” to suggest
that Australia should have its own particular game of
cricket. Dave’s idea is to ring the batting team off at 3
p.m. and let the other side have an innings, so that the
public might study the batting and fielding of both teams
in one day. It is hard to say whether players or public
would be the sooner sick of such an idiotic innovation.
The tail-end of a strong team might play in half-a-dozen
successive matches without getting a show to bat at all,
inasmuch as weaker sides would lose twenty wickets for
fewer runs than were scored to the first seven of their
opponents. Any attempt to meet this objection by some-
times putting in the “tail” first, and keeping back the
“ cracks” until the last, would knock half the sense out
of cricket. The public, for their part, wouldn’t roll up on
the possible chance of seeing Giffen carry out his bat for
a “ duck.” Twenty likely instances might be suggested
to prove the absurdity of Dave Scott’s windy proposition,
which he hangs on to the utterly false argument that
people in England have more time than the Australians
to follow a big match for three or four days on a stretch.
Nobody knows better than Scott that matches never
extend over four days in England, that Australian batting
has become slower than the “home” play, and that a
little ginger is the only thing required to make cricket
out here (and more particularly in Victoria) as palatable
to the public as it was before the over-rated and conceit-
saturated “giants” of eight years ago us'xl to drag out
their stupid gate-money matches into thejifth day.—F.E.
# ' * *
The worst Victorian Eleven on record has met
the best English one on record.
« • *
If George Lohmann is the best all-round
cricketer in England, as alleged by fie British papers,
then George Giffen is easily champion of the world.
The English cricketing team is the finest field-
ing crowd which has ever visited Australia, but M'Gregor,
the much-trumpeted wicket-keeper, is inferior to Black-
ham and Jarvis. The latter is in wonderful form and
stood behind the wickets all day without a single sundry
being recorded, whilst 323 runs were made !
* * *
The Englishmen scored 16—of which Grace
hit 14— from the first two overs bowled against them on
the M.C.C. ground. That’s what W.G. means by “putting
more life into the game.” He and Abel scored 91 runs in
65 minutes.
* * »
That four o’clock adjournment for drinks will
disappear from big matches on the Melbourne Ground
henceforth and for ever. Grace put his bat down upon
the ancient practice and killed it.
# * #
Tom Garrett will have his first stand-down in
the match against the Englishmen. “Every dog has his
day ” and Mr. Garrett has had a longer day than most
men.
• * •
When Grace had scored 14 in the recent Mel-
bourne cricket match he was missed by Morris in the
slips. Every run after that made by W.G. must have been
like a stab in Morris’ side. That catch would have at least
saved a one-innings defeat.
• • *
It is stated that the reason why Mr. George
Seale, undoubtedly the champion amateur boxer of Aus-
tralia, never faces the music now, is that Minister Bruce
Smith assured him that should he fight in earnest any
more he would have to leave the N.S.W. Government
service.
• * *
Some of the papers talk about “Gentleman
George ” Dawson having been reinstated in his former
position as champion light-weight, by Williams’ neglect
to make another match. This may lie technically true,
but it is arrant nonsense all the same, as nobody doubts
that Williams could thump Dawson every time.
* * #
Should Jackson and Slavin really meet, Slavin
will win. Before Slavin left for England he said to an old
Brisbane patron : “ I will win or lose my fights in six or
eight rounds.” His longest fight up to now has been of
nine rounds; the others have been ail less than six.
Slavin is a grim fighter by nature. His soul rejoices in
the shock of biffs and uppercuts. His constitution would
enable him to endure three times the belting Jackson
could.
* * *
Sullivan has posted money for a match with
Slavin, to come off in September or October next. As
well might he fix it for the beginning of next century.
By the way, it does not appear to have struck anybody,
up to date, that Sullivan and Slavin are really the same
name, the latter being a mere corruption of the former,
as Moss is of Moses. Perhaps, however, J. L. Sullivan
will yet find that Patrick Francis’ name conies from Slay,
with a big S. We shouldn’t be surprised.
# * *
The Bulletin is now convinced that if Sullivan
is ever to be tackled, black Samuels, the fast sprinter, and
not Slavin is the man for the job.
# * *
Pugilistic circles have been much exercised over
the primi-facie shabby action of the Sydney Amateur
Gymnastic Club in giving Murphy £4O and Burge nil in
connection with the recent mill, which was declared “ no
fight.” The writer is under the impression that Burge
would only enter the ring under the condition that the
fight should be “ to a finish ” pure and simple, and he has
only himself to blame if he now does not receive any-
thing, for had the referee possessed the power to declare
in favour of one or (be other on “points” when he
thought the fight should he stopped, there is no knowing
that the “go ’• might have been awarded to Murphy, who
,lid all the fighting, and then Burge would have received
the loser’s portion of the purse.

# * *
The sixteenth interprovincial boat race, which
took place last Saturday on the Yarra, was a procession
of three, with Queensland three lengths ahead of A.o. vv.,
and Victoria toiling alone in the extreme rear. Possibly
there was too much massive beef in the hinder mats.
The Queensland crew is said to have averaged lOst. 3|ib.,
the Cornstalks list. 12£1b., and the Victorians list,
which seems a serious difference in bulk. Queensland
got ahead almost at the outset, rowed at a tremendous
bat all through, and was never overtaken At the ferry
the Northern men were one length ahead; at Spottiswoode
Point they were two lengths in front; and after that the
interest centred in the frantic struggle between N.h.v .
and Victoria for second place. N.S. Wales gained it by a
stupendous effort, and the Southerners came in with their
tail down at the very end. Time, 18min. 45sec.
* # *
Bowler Turner’s phenomenal “ screw ” is due in
part to his remarkably strong grip. He can crack a
walnut between his finger and thumb.
# # *
G’Naroo was named by J. O. Inglis after a
water-hole on his Mount Margaret (Q.) station. The
name signifies “ good.”
# * •
An Australian cricket record. 1° 1878, in a
match between Rix’s Creek and Warkworth (N.S.W.),
Harry Grainger took ten wickets in two overs—only three
runs being scored. In his second destructive over, every
ball upset stumps. In another match, in which Grainger
bowled, the batters were all disposed of for four rues.
This phenomenal (bush) bowler is now keeping an hotel at
Singleton.
# * *
Ladv Superior, the 20 to 1 winner of the Sand-
hurst Cun, was backed for small money by many punters
who remembered what a good race the mare ran in the
V.R.C. Oaks of 1889.
* # *
George Barrett, one of the three leading Eng-
lish iockeys, lately rode 33 races in succession without a
win. A backer of his, commencing with a pound on the
double-and-quits principle, would thus have lost
£850,000,000 sterling, or rather more than the fingnsn
national debt.
Messrs. Raphael Tuck and Sons, of London,
Paris and New York, have sent along a large
collection of Christmas-eards—all of them beauti-
ful works of art, and most of them calculated to
inspire holy thoughts and noble aspirations. The
majority are decorated with delicately-tinted
angels seraphs, roosters, flowers, infants, rose-
buds and things of that description, and in all
The Bulletin's wide experience of angels, and
the like, it lias never seen anything superior to
these. If it meets as good a sample waiting tor
it when it becomes an ethereal publication in the
fields of Asphodel, it will be perfectly contented.

Tales of the Old Regime.
No, XVII .—The Felicitous Reminiscences of
Scourger James.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“Now God made man, but the devil made
the Convict Scourger. Either the devil or the
devil’s own son, the System’s Official.”
That was the substance of what Oibbety Joe
used to say, and Gibbety Joe had a right to
express an opinion on the point. For he had
received at the cat-ends several thousand lashes at
sundry times and in different places.
# * *
George Augustus Sala in his “Echoes of
the Week” published in the Melbourne Argus
and the New York Herald, said Joe’s tally was
7000. Which was a cruel libel on the Regime. It
is shameful to libel the dead, and when the dis-
tinguished London journalist exaggerated that
wherewith the men of Old Time had blessed
Gibbety, he wronged them scandalously. There
was no excuse for Sala—none in the least, doe’s
voice was not such a mumble that it did not ring
through the white-washed barn to No. 15 bed—
Joe’s was in No. 12, on the other side—and the
occupant of No. 15 heard Joe say, plain enough,
“ Six thousan’l ’ed, all told.” And if G.A.S.
put 7000 down in his note-book, he was inex-
cusably careless, for his carelessness led him to
commit the heinous offence of circulating the
statement that the total of Joe’s tally was the
greater number when he should have reported
what he had been told —the lesser. There are
some things that will not bear exaggeration, and
when the excess does injury to the reputations of
the dead, well, the affair is very bad.
“An’ ’twar wery wrong of him, sir,” whined
No. 15-bed, “to say wot war not true. An’
he’ll go down to hell of a surety, sir. I flicked
Joe his last hundred an’ fifty, sir, an’ I ought to'
know, for not to say naught of his tellin’ the man
hisself as he lay in that bed there —he was No. 12,
sir—not to say naught o’ that, as I. heard him my-
self, I had it from the Cora’dant. An’ he scorned
a lie like anythink, did Com’dant. An’ I say ’tis
a shame to slander us so —’twarn’t seven thousand,
’twas on’y six—’tis a shame, it is, a blarsted
shame.”
“James ! ” in grieved tones.
“ O mem, may God forgive me for a-swearin’,
but it makes my blood boil to hear the Systim
libulled ! ’Twarn’t seven thousand —’twar on’y
six !” And James James, occupant of No. 15
bed, in a Tasmanian Invalid Depot, squeezed out
some tear-like drops. One of his hearers thought
his moist grief was due to his shame at the
outrage on the System’s fame ; another, to his fear
that his oath had offended the former, a kindly,
conscientious lady-agent of the City Mission, and
that he would in consequence gain a bad mark,
and lose his hardly-earned repute for sanctity,
and the extra tobacco and dainties which his
saint-hood brought him. For James James was a
model convert —a very brand plucked from the
burning—and his conversion had been given
seventeen lines of small-pica in the Mission’s last
annual report. He, retired floggerand descendant
of the devil, was yet anxious to die in the odour
of holiness and genuine Barrett’s-twist, and now
here in his righteous indighatiort at the casting
by a scribbling globe-trotter of a disgraceful
imputation on the System which had been a solid
friend to him, lie had back-slidden into a warmth
of language quite unfitting a recent convert and
newly-born saint.
There can be no question, it was hard upon
him. Sala was inexcusably wrong in giving
way to a professional tendency to exaggera-
tion. There is only one thing to be said in
extenuation of his fault. He may have thought
that as matters went in the days of the Old
Regime there was precious little difference
between seven thousand lashes and six thousand.
Perhaps there was not. The man who had
received six might have been presented with the
additional thousand without doing him much
additional harm, always provided that the ten
hundred followed upon, say, three or four
hundred. After that preliminary dose the extra
lot would mean just another pint or two of blood
on the ground. Pew men felt pain after the four-
hundredth lash. If you want to know why, ask
the nearest doctor.
# * *
That James James, per ship Hercules,
having drawn upon the vocabulary of his unre-
generate days should suffer, \yas right and proper,
and therefore we went away and left him to be
prayed with by the City Mission lady. But in
two hours’ time we went back to his bedside and
upon the coverlet we spread six figs of tobacco, a
new briar-wood pipe, a pot of jam, and three
ounces of jujubes. No child has a sweeter tooth
than an old transport who has only two or three
teeth left in his gums.
“ These are yours, James .”
“ Oh, thank yer, sir ! May God bless .”
“None o’ that, my friend. I’m not connected
with the City Mission. They are yours on one
condition.”
“Yes, sir—oh, yes, sir!”
“That you get up and act the scourger—not
merely tell me of your doings as a scourger, but
act them ! Do you understand !”
The jaws of No. 15’s neighbours fell open wider
and their wrinkles multiplied with ghostly laugh-
ter. James’ predicament was as the savour of a
Sunday dinner -to his neighbours. Like most
saints, who are also hypocrites, he was heartily
detested by those who saw most of him. There-
fore, they waited, amused, for the reply they knew
would come.
“But I can’t, sir. I’m bed-ridden wi’ the
r’eumatiz ! I can’t act a scourger !”
“Rheumatism is bad, James, but there’s a
worse thing than rheumatism, James. And that
is hell, James —and liars go to hell, don’t they,
James?”
“ Ye-e-s, sir!”
“Now, James, I’m not a City Missionary, and
therefore you won’t get anything by telling lies to
me. You’re not bed-ridden, James, for all your
78 years—l’ve been looking up your record, you
see—and you’ll get up and show me how you
swung the cats or you don’t touch any of those
good things.”
He moved as if to rise, and then stopped.
“Wot if the Super, came, sir?” he whispered.
“ He thinks I’m very bad.”
“ You’re afraid of your special ration, James ?”
“That’s it, sir ! I’m an invalid on speshal.”
The wretch winked.
‘ ‘ And you think you have taken in the Super.
Why, he told me you were shamming rheu-
matism
“ Did he, sir ? An’ yet he keeps me on speshal
diet! Well !”
“ Yes, he does. It’s rather surprising, but he
does it for a gpod reason. He says you’re so sure
of a bad time hereafter in spite of your conversion
that he wants to make your last days here pretty
comfortable. So you see there is no use pretending
any more. Get up and show me how you used the
cats- ”
The old sinner was out of bed before the sen-
tence was completed, and as he dressed begged for
a jujube. “Jest one, sir! On'y one whiles I
thinks of wot to say.”
The onlookers caught one glimpse of his back.
Evidently he himself had been under punishment,
but equally certain was it that his scourger had
been a duffer. There were no cicatrices of long
weals ; only twenty or so dots were depressed on
the outer-skin, and told that the cats had gathered
unto themselves “ bits ” and no more than “ bits.”
Some of his neighbours sighed as they noted the
scarcely-roughened surface. The Fates were not
always so kind to their malignant instruments.
“You’re lookin’ at my back, fellows ! ” grinned
James. “ A’most a clear skin, ain’t it? ”
“ That’s your shame, James,” in a passionful
shout, exclaimed another invalid. “ There was
no ’souse, was there, chaps, for a man to take
scourger’s billet unless he was a-driven to it by
th’ System ? ”
The paupers growled an ominous assent and
James looked uncomfortably for a moment. Then
he made a clutch at some more jujubes that were
offered him, and put the nasty thought out of
mental sight as to what his bed-neighbours would
say when the stranger had gone and the wards-
man’s back was turned. Even seventy —and
eighty—year olds, when they spent their youth
under the tuition of the Old Regime, retain be-
neath the placid exterior the power to vent the
Baresark rage. And as it is not good for a soldier
to rejoin his regiment when his wounds are in the
rear, so it was bad for a retired scourger to show
a “clear” back. With a “clear” skin, why was
he flogger? That was a question some of his
fellow-paupers meant to ask him by-and-bye.
# # #
“It was at the Harbour that I w r as promoted
to scourger, sir—in ’2B or ’29—an’ I was a younker
—twenty or so—l misremember exactly, an’ I
took the billet to save ray own skin.”
Here his brother-relics growled in a threatening
under-voice, “We knowed it—-we knowed it!”
“To save my own skin —an’ ye’d ha’all done
the same, for ’twar a double-cat, war the Harbour
cat, an’ I war a weak younker, an’ wot little flesh
I had I wanted to keep a-stickin’ on my bones.
Flesh is better on your bones than on the ground.
An’ ’tain’t sweet to see a patch o’ red dampness on
the soil, an’ know as it’s your wine.
“It war a double-cat—the rogues’ cat—nine
tails on’y, but nine knots ’stead o’ three on each
tail, an’ each tail war wot the croaker called a
bi-fold or a tri-fold —the whipcord war twist twice
or thrice. An’ the cracker of each tail war some-
times a wire point the length of a pin, may be, an’
sometimes a bit o’ cord stiffened with glue an’
salt. Mostly the cracker war the bit o’ cord, for
it took a smarty to fix the wire so it shouldn’t
drop out, an’ some scourgers never could. I never
could, master, an’ yer people an’ Confidants ha’
thought me a bit of a dab in my time.”
“So you couldn’t fix the wire? That was un-
fortunate !”
“Rather, sir, for if I’d been able to do that, I
wouldn’t ha’ got into trouble with the men at the
Harbour. ’Twar this way : I war a-handlin’ a
cat one day when I saw as the wire tips were
a-givin’. Now, ’twar no use stoppin’—the man’s
dose was on’y forty-nine.”—
“ Forty-nine? Was that to save the trouble of
calling the doctor?”
“ Yer hit it, sir ! Fifty ’ud been ’bliged to call
an’ so ’twas made one less—to save him trouble,
sir. So ’twarn’t, I say, no use stoppin’, an’—an’—
the bit o’ wire dropped orf—on the ground, I
thought. An’, of course, it didn’t matter.”
“ Not in the least, of course.”
“ On’y, when the pris’ner went into hospital,
he didn’t get better ’soon’s he should, an’ then he
got po’rish, an’ an abscess come along, an’ the
croaker couldn’t cure him, though, p’r’ups, as he
war on’y a pris’ner, he didn’t try wery hard. An’
when he died, for die he did, although it war on’y
forty-nine, the croaker opened him—for skientific
pupposes, sir, he ses—an’ the wire war in a wital
part. That made th’ chaps blow on me. Though
how I was to blame, I never see. No one war to
blame, on’y the System, for a-usin’ cats with wire-
crackers.
“No one war to blame. An’ the crawler died.’’
* x # »
“So when they ‘blew’ upon you, I sup-
pose the Commandant sent you to Hobart Town? ’
“ Yes, sir, ’tvvas in ’2!) I went up, by the old
Waterloo. An’ in ’32 I was sent to the new settle-
ment at Port Arthur, under Rossell. Ye’ve heard
of Rossell —him as flogged his own bastard boy to
death ? # —but I had no hand in that. An’ there
I grew more an’ more of a dab. I larnt to cross-
cut there. So !”
The Super., for experiment, had lent us an
ancient five-tailed scourge—one of the pattern
invented by Ernest Augustus Slyde, godson of
the Duke of Cumberland, sometime Police Magis-
trate of Sydney, and distinguished scoundrelf—
and this pretty toy James James cuddled lovingly
before he combed out the tails.
“So!” he sail. “So!” He disobeyed the"
apostolic precept and beat the air—artistically.
“ Yer begin quick, sir, an’ light—so! An’y’r
slow down as you get heavier—y’ know it hurts
’em the more the slower the blows. An’ you lets
the cuts fall on the shoulders first, an’ then come
lower an’ lower. An’ at the tally, if you’re a
cross-cutter, y’ change hands if you’re strong in
the left arm, and if you ain't, why you on’y turn
your wrist. It’s purtier, sir, to cross ’em with the
wrist on’y—purtier an’ nicer, ’cos almost anybody
can cross the cuts if y’ take both hands to it, but
to use on’y one hand fust one way, an’ then t’other
that’s reel difficult.”
“ Ah, you’d the makings of a great artist in
you, James, hadn’t you ? It’s only the great
artist who aspires after excellence that attempts
the difficult.”
“ I don’t quite follow you, sir, but that’s wot
Cap’n Rous—l hearn tell he became Admiral
arter, him as war a great-racin’ man—funny,
warn’t it, he should be so fond of the bosses, an’
him nothink but a sailor—”
“ Well, what did he say ?”
“ I war an artist, sir—a reel artist!”
“Why, how was that? What had Captain
Rous to do with you ?”
“It war this way. His ship, the —the—”
Recollection failed him for a moment, and so his
interlocutor thought he would assist him—into a
trap. The one almost certain method of detect-
ing whether an ex-convict is lying or not in a
narration of old-times, is to suggest at the first
lacuna in his story some imaginary name or date
or incident, and if the fellow is a liar, he will in-
stantly adopt the suggested “fact.” The dodge,
if not quite moral, is efficacious. It was tried on
this occasion.
“ Yes, let’s see what was his ship ? The Erebus,
wasn’t it ? ” Now the Erebus was Ross’s ship.

“ No,’twarn’t the Erebus. That
an’ she was in company with the Terror ? ”
“ The Beagle—was it the Beagle? ”
“No —’twarn’t the Beagle. Drat it, now, wot
war it?”
“ The Rainbow ?”
The felloW s eyes lit up with a gleam that went
bail for his veracity. “ The Rainbow ’twar, you re
right, sir.”
“ Well, get on with your story. What had you
to do with the Rainbow ? ’
“ VVe had just dropped anchor in the Cove the
Waterloo, I mean —an’ the Rainbow war on’y a
couple of cables’length from us, an’ she hailed our
skipper to send two scourgers on board. Me an
’nuther war sent orf.”
“ What did a King’s ship want with doggers
when she’d her boatswain’s mates ?”
“We wondered at that, an’ when we got on
board we soon knew. Captain Rous had, sure as
I’m here, sir, made up his min’ to flog the w’ole
ship’s company, petty-orf’cers an’ all. When the
ship sailed into the Derwent there ought to ha
been a relief crew an’ transport there, for the
Rainbow war to stop on the station herself. An
there war no relief crew an’ no transport, an’ the
crew was as rusty as this here cat. An’ the
bosen’s mates, nor the bosen, nor the marine, ’ud
lift a whip, an’ that’s the reason we war sent for.
An’ we guv’em all a dozen a-piece, and the war-
runt-officers two dozen, and the bosen’s mates
three dozen, for they war ’tickerly rebelyas, Cap’n
Rous said. But the bosen’s mates were put on
the gratin’s last, an’ they got orf lightest arter
all! ”
“ How was that? Were you tired?”
“ Scarcely a bit when ’twas all oyer ! That
’twasn’t the reason. It was the slip’riness of the
decks —we couldn’t stand at the last. It’s not in
natur’, sir, as two-hunderd men could get them
lashes, an’not leave traces. Not that all spurted
jooce ! ” He stopped —to laugh.
“ What was the naval cat like? It was different
from this ? ”
“ Oh, lor, yes ! Three feet o’ rope tarred an
resined—eighteen inch rove an’ boun’ round
with sail-thread, an’ ’t’other eighteen inch in six
tails sometimes, an’ sometimes nine, with three
knots on each tail. It war the easiest to use
long way nicer than the Settlement cat the
Harbour cat. And when I’d done on the Rain-
bow an’ got my glass of grog, an’ Cap’n Rous’d
told me I war an artist —lor! sir, how he larfed
as he said it! —I arxed his Honor for the cat, an’
he guv it me, quite pleasunt-likean’ gen’el’manly.
An’ I kep’ it for a long time, an’ used it at Port
Arthur once or twice till Capt’n Boote— him as
followed Dr. Rossell, sir—said as its use war quite
illegal, ’cos it war more distructive than our cat —
‘it war ’tensifyin’ punishmunt beyond the demands
of justice ’ —them war his wery words, sir—’cos a
dozen with a man-o’-war’s cat war ekal to fifty
with ours, an’ fifty with the man-o’-war cut a man
up more’n two hundred of System’s floggin’.'’
“Well, that was genuinely kind of Captain
Boote !”
“ That’s as it may be. Wait till ye hear all.
“It war Monday. Com’dant Rossell used to
flog most o’ Sunday, but Boote was more religious
—‘it didn’t give a chap, who was at the triangles
at eight o’clock, much chance of attendin’church
at ten in a good frame o’ mind ’ —them are his
words agen, sir—an’ so he made Monday his
princ’pal floggin’ day, an’ John Price, sir, he liked
Monday best, too, scourgers war flasher, he used
to say, after Sunday’s rest but there, I’m
ramblin’—wot war I goin’ to say ?”
“About Boote prohibiting the use of the naval
cat as illegal ?”
“Ah, that war it! Well, one Monday I’d
flogged a man with the man-o’-war tool, an’ as
he’d been some’ut of a chum o’ mine, I warn’t as
heavy as might ha’ been. That’s one ’vantage
of the man-o’-war cat. Being shorter in the handle
an’ length o’ tail, yer stand in closer, an’ if
yer like can dodge the orf’cer’s eyes as is
watchin’yer. Catch the tails so” —he gathered
up the tails of the thing he held in his hand at
the root, where they sprang from the heft—“an’
you on’y let the ends catch on the man. That
war wot I’d did that mornin’, an’ tho’ the fellow’d
suffered some’ut, yet still ’twarn’t much of a
floggin’, I’ll admit. Now Boote was a-standin’
aside, an’ could see better’n the chief-constable
who was taliyin’, an’ he said when last tally was
called, the “ use of that cat ain’t legul, Scourger ;
it’s more distructive than our cat —it’s tensifyin’
pun’shment, that’s wot ’tis—no, I can't ’low it!”
An’ then, ’cos the ship’s cat was illegal, what d’ye
think he made me do, to krect the illegul’ty ? ”
“ Can’t say, am sure ! ”
“ He made me guv the pris’ner his dose of fifty
over agen with the Settlement-cat. That’s wot he
called ‘ krecting the illegul’ty.’ He was a stickler
for doin’ things in order was Cap’n Boote. ’Cos
the first lot warn’t legul, he guv ’im the second.”
# * *
“ Now, what about Gibbety Joe ? You
gave him, you say, his last hundred and fifty?
What was that for, and why was he called Gib-
bety ?"’
“It war in the ’4B, sir, an’ things war a-gettin’
moral-like —not so hard for the pris’ners an’
Gov’mnnt labour. But I war still offi’shal scourger
an’ doin’ well, busy or not busy. Two-an’-six a
day an’ a double rashun warn't to be sneezed at.
“Well, I war flogger at Cam’eltown. An’one
fine day, a court day, who should come along for
pun’shmunt but Gibbety Joe. He war named
Gibbety Joe ’cos he war the last man sentenced to
bo gibbetted on Hunter’s Island —the ol’ wharf at
Hobart Town runs out to the Island, sir, for ’taint
an island no longer—it’s filled in .”
“I know all about it. Go on! Why was he
sentenced, and why was he reprieved ?”
“He war sentenced for doin’ (killing) his man,
an’ he war ’preeved ’cos he war Gov’munt cox’n,
an’ no finer boat hand war ’vailable. But ’preevin’
him didn’t do him no manner of good, for he war
always in trouble —he war so high-spirited an’
such a tongue I never seed in all my born days.
He never paid no respeck to pussons, as Cat’chism
ses, an’ he war rude all round.
“Well, upCam’eltown-way, he’d been assigned.
He ought to ha’ had freedom long afore, but from
a-bein’ in trouble always, he had piled up more
sentence’n all other hands on the place put
together. It ’ud have taken eight or nine
ord’nary Gov’ment men to sarve his times.”
“ Was he a violent customer?”
“ No, never; never struck a blow arter the first
one which killed his man. But sech a tongue !
He gave nobody rest with it. An’ ’twar for
makin’ free with his master, a-tellin’ him that had
every one their own, their posishuns ’ud ha’ been
reversed, that he was in for the pun’shmunt as I
guv him.
“ One - hundred - an’ • fifty in quarter - minute
strokes ”
“ What/” Forty minutes, while the strokes
should not have taken more than three, was
punishment worthy of the Inquisition.
“Oh, I’m speakin’ true, sir! Forty mmuteSj
an’ a rest betwixt each cut.
“ I cuts him high—so! An’ I cuts him low —
so ! An’ then I was bid to cut him on his legs.
He was all raw (fresh) on the legs, but ’twarn’t
that wot made him yell.”
“He yelled? I shouldn’t have thought he was
the fellow to yell ? ”
“That war his dodge—that's why he hadn’t
died long afore from his hoggin’s. It war ’cos he
knew that th’ cove who cried an’ yelled out didn’t
feel so much as the fellow who played game an’
bold—the croakers used to say that the strain on
the nerves to keepin’ the mouth shut were nearly
fatal—an’ not that he war a coward that he yelled.
He war no coward ! He’d took his five'thousand-
eight-fifty an’ lived 1 That warn’t a coward’s
work.
“ Well, he yelled, an’ yelled. Bub when last
tally war called,' he stops the yellin’ an’
shouted ”
“Domino! Domino! Was it Domino? ”
James’s pauper-hearers had been listening
breathlessly to the recital, and it was from between
their sunken lips that the cry had come. The
Valhalla of convict mythology was populated with
the men who cried “ Domino !” after flogging. It
was the hero’s stamp.
* * *
“ Yes, ’twar ‘Domino !’ But ’twarn’t all.
Arter I’d cast him loose he flung his shirt over his
shoulder, an’, marchin’ past the Police-Magistrate
who’d stopped to watch pun’shmunt, Joe snapped
his fingers in his face and cried, as bold as brass :
‘ There, that’s how much I think of your floggings,
old shaver !’ ”
“Hooray, hooray!” The white-washed barns
echoed to the phantom-cheer which gasped through
toothless gums. James seized the opportunity to
extract the sweetness from another jujube. Then
he went on to his conclusion.
“But for all his pluck, that Hoggin’broke his
heart an’ spirit. He’d turned to the cells, when
he looked up, an’ saw standin’ in the gateway of
the yard two ladies —the Magistrate’s lady an’
anuther. They’d been lookin’ on.
“Joe saw them —stared —turned grave-cloth
white —an’ ses : ‘ Good God ! them wimmen
heard me a-yellin’, an’ they must ha’_ thought as I
was a crawler !’ His head fell on his breast, an
as ’twar, sir, he never lifted it no more. He
couldn’t a-bear to be thought a coward by wimmen,
an’, o’ course, the wimmen didn’t know as he
warn’t no coward. An’ his pride war hurt. The
’Thorities never had no more trouble with him
arter that—he lived forty year arter, but he war
always sick. He had no more pluck, an’ no more
spirit. An’ he didn’t get no consolashun from the
fac’ as he owned the record for the colony, sir.
No one topped his six thousan’ —none. + An’ now,
sir, can I have the pipe an’ baccy ? ”
* # •
For his reward he had them —and some-
thing else. Thoughtlessly, when we went out
of the Invalid Ward, we left behind us the
cat the Super, had lent us for the experiment.
James James’ bed : neighbours displayed that
night what, considering their age and debility,
was an astonishing and commendable strength of
flagellation. They resented that “ blear ’’-skin.

was Michael Keane’s, ex-soldier, about eight thousand
lashes.

“ I had occasion,” writes “ Pentecost ” in
Twentieth Century, “tospend last Sunday week
in Toronto, Canada, and to see how quiet a city
of two hundred thousand inhabitants can be made
by religious rule. Not a horse-car is allowed to
run. Not a store or shop of any kind is allowed
to be open. Not a newspaper is allowed to be
printed. Only one railroad train is allowed to
pass through the town. Not a postage-stamp, or
cigar, or glass of sodawater can be bought. Few
persons are seen on the streets, except in going to
and from church. On Sunday the city appears
deserted of inhabitants. If one is poor, no oppor-
tunities for pleasure to be had. If one is rich,
however, he may drive about in a carriage,
or if a guest at a hotel ho may order
drinks to his room. Religions rule has
made Sunday a terror to the poor, unless one
happens to enjoy going to church, walking about
the quiet streets, reading or sleeping. There are
104 churches in the city, all untaxed. There are
but 106 churches in New York, with two million
and a quarter inhabitants. Clergymen and lay
churchmen rule Toronto and keep it as lifeless as
a log. There are 13 wards in the city, every one
of which is named after a saint. Happily, I was
able to get back to Buffalo before the dismal Sab-
bath was over. There, in a hotel, I found an
orchestra discoursing the sweetest secular music-
waltzes, gavottes, boleros, melodies —while the
guests were dining. The contrast was deliciously
in favour of the States as against priest-ridden
Canada.
THE DOCTORS SHOOK THEIR HEADS.
“ I would give a million dollars for a stomach,”
said a wealthy American recently. Now, a
million dollars (£200,000) is a deal of money, but he
was not able to buy the article even at that price.
Mr. Thomas Gaskin, of No. 3, Poplar Row,
Darley Abbey, near Derby, is not so rich as many,
being a gardener, but he was ready enough to give
all he had for one thing he hadn’t got. Here is
his story, and he shall tell it in his own way. Mr.
Gaskin goes on to say : “Up to eight years ago I
had never lost an hour’s work through sickness.
In December, 1882, whilst living at Beeston, near
Nottingham, my appetite fell away, I had dread-
ful pain after eating, a bad taste in my mouth,
which would fill with water and slime, the latter
so thick and sticky that I often had to remove it
with my fingers. My chest used to ‘ fill up ’ and
my throat would swell so I couldn’t button my
shirt at the neck. I vomited a great deal,
chiefly a yellowish-green phlegm. I had a racking
cough, and would cough and strain till I bled at
the nose, and the blood rushed out of my ears. I
became exhausted and short of breath and had to
call the doctor, and was in bed five or six weeks.
“I then went as out-patient to the Nottingham
Hospital, and after attending there for six months
was very little better. I got so thin that I was
like a ghost walking about, and my friends said it
was all up with me. For eighteen months I kept
getting better, then worse, and at last the doctors
recommended me to try a change of air. So I
came to Darley Abbey and my former master gave
me an indoor recommendation to the Derbyshire
Infirmary, Derby. I found, however, that all the
beds in the hospital were full, and I saw my club
doctor and also two doctors at Derby. The first
one said, ‘ You are in a galloping consumption, and
must die ; ’ the other doctors, after sounding my
lungs and chest, said nothing, but shook their heads.
“Getting a trifle better my old master allowed
me to do what little I could in his gardens and
grounds; and some days I worked a little, and
then would be away for a few days. Things went
on like this until December, 1888, when I caught
cold, got very short of breath, and broke down
completely, I was always coughing and spitting
up phlegm, sometimes as much as a teacupful at
once, and was afraid to go to sleep for fear the
phlegm ivould choke me. At this time the whites
of my eyes turned yellow, and my eyes were
bloodshot. My lips and mouth were dry and
parched and I often broke out into a cold, clammy
sweat. My tongue was like a piece of brown
leather, and I could keep nothing down. I lost
over a stone weight in three weeks.
“ I managed to get up for a few hours at a time,
but was so weak I had to take hold of the
furniture to move a few yards. I was always
tired and weary, and sitting or lying, was never
at ease and got no real rest. Too weak to read I
often sat for hours looking at my hands—nothing
but skin and bone, and was so nervous I couldn’t
bear the least noise, and the children at play quite
upset me.
“ The doctor kept on attending me, but he said,
* I can do you no good ; I can only patch you up,
because your lungs are diseased.' I then gave over
taking his medicine and tried everything I could
hear of. One thing was a cough cure from London
that cost 225. a bottle. But nothing did any good,
and my friends who came to see me said, ‘ Well,
you won't cough much longer, you have merely to
pt'eparefor another world.'
“ About the middle of March, 1889, a neighbour
named Joseph Tatham, who works in the paper
mills at Darley Abbey, said to me, ‘ Tom, did you
ever try Mother Seigel's Syrup ?’ and told me how
he had been cured after being in the hospital. He
spoke so much in praise of this medicine I sent
my wife to Derby and got a bottle at Mr. Smith’s
Stores in Victoria Street. After the first dose I
had an evacuation black as ink, and vomited.
Then I fell asleep and felt rested when I awoke.
After taking the medicine for three days my
breathing got easier, my food kept down, and in a
few days more I was able to go out for a short
walk. By the end of a fortnight I went to see my
master, who asked what had wrought the
improvement, and I told him, as I tell every one,
that Mother Seigel’s Syrup had saved my life. I
commenced work at once, and have never lost a
day’s work since.
“ Of course my strength did not return all at
once, but after taking Seigel’s Syrup for six
months I was strong as ever in my life and have
continued so ever since.”
In giving his consent to the publication of this
remarkable statement, Mr. Gaskin requests us to
refer all doubters to him or to Mr. Keetley, Head
Gardener, Darley Abbey, who is acquainted with
all the facts alleged. Mr, Gaskin has lived at
Darley Abbey 22 years and bears an excellent
reputation.
Now, what was the complaint from which he
suffered ? Not galloping consumption, certainly
(as one of the doctors thought), or he would have
galloped into his grave in less than three months ,
instead>of growing worse for seven years. His
complaint Was chronic inflammatory dyspepsia
(or chronic gastric catarrh), which has lung
symptoms like consumption, for which it is so
often ignorantly mistaken and wrongly treated.
When the right remedy, Mother Seigel’s Syrup,
was used the disease yielded, as all diseases of the
digestive tract do yield to that magnificent pre-
paration. We heartily congratulate Mr. Gaskin
on his recovery, and commend the facts of his
case to all thinking people.

When the explorer Stuart was travelling
across the Australian continent, he lost a bay cob
at Kirschner’s Ponds. This nag only died recently,
and must have been about 40 years old.

Writes a correspondent : —In Nicholas v.
Howells, the biggest equity case ever heard in
Tasmania, Henry Dobson has briefed himself and
two other counsel for plaintiff. His opening ad-
dress occupied three days and a half. Though
admitted to practice as a barrister and solicitor 27
years ago, and though senior partner in the largest
Hobart firm, this is his first appearance. As he
entered Parliament about four months ago, and
now leads the Opposition, it is currently believed
that he is testing his capabilities for the Attorney-
Generalship. By the way, this is the case for
appearance in which Goldsmith, of Melbourne, is
retained by some of the defendants at <£40 a day.
The case has already occupied eight days and
defendant’s case has not begun.

Had the original programme of official
appointments to the N.S.W. executive at Chicago
Exhibition been carried out, a delightful little
family party would have had a good time. Mr.
Wilkins is or was in Oscar Meyer s Sydney office,
and the pair received the chief posts. A
friend of Meyer’s, a well-known pressman,
formerly attached to the Age, was to have got a
billet as working secretary, and to have joined
Oscar in Chicago.

A first dividend of 4s. in the £ is to be
paid to V.D.L. Bank creditors on 1st. Dec. This
means the release of about £150,000, which will
be a veritable godsend in the present congested
state of the Tasmanian money market.
Gladstone’s recent visit to Newcastlc-on-
Tyne and his prediction that the Liberals will win
hands-down at the next general election, recalls
his other prophecy delivered at Newcastle during
the American civil war. “ We may expect with
certainty,” said Gladstone, —
The success of the Southern States as far as regards
effecting their separation from the North. I, for my part,
cannot but believe that that event is as certain as anything
future and contingent may be.
“ As Mr. Gladstone had before publicly and
exultingly stated that Mr. Jefferson Davis ‘ had
made an army, had made a navy, and had made a
nation,’possibly, in his confident prediction, the
wish was father to the thought,” remarks an
English paper.

Jonathan Hutchinson, the great medical
specialist, in narrating his personal experience
concerning alcohol and tea, lately said that beer
made his boots too tight and tea made them too
large, causing contraction of the blood-vessels
both of the feet and of the head, just as beer
caused dilatation. He held that there was no
reason to believe, in spite of the showy statistics
of some insurance offices, that teetotallers attained
greater longevity, or enjoyed their lives better,
than those who took alcohol in moderate quanti-
ties. Of course, the difficulty is to know how
much is a “moderate quantity.”
